Attention Crisis: How leaders can fix focus and happiness in an AI Era -psychologist Jonathan Haidt

World Economic Forum(1.1M)·22:23·1.5K views·2026-05-04
Jonathan HaidtSocial Psychologist and Professor, NYU
TL;DRHaidt argues that happiness is not a product of material prosperity or internal mindset alone, but emerges from the quality of relationships "between" ourselves and our world.
Social psychologist Jonathan Haidt warns that social media and AI are dismantling the foundations of human happiness—relational connection, meaningful work, and shared truth—requiring urgent regulatory accountability and a collective return to "effortful" living to...

TL;DR: Social psychologist Jonathan Haidt warns that social media and AI are dismantling the foundations of human happiness—relational connection, meaningful work, and shared truth—requiring urgent regulatory accountability and a collective return to "effortful" living to prevent the destruction of human capital.

The Foundations of Happiness vs. AI

Haidt argues that happiness is not a product of material prosperity or internal mindset alone, but emerges from the quality of relationships "between" ourselves and our world.

  • The Relational Triad: True flourishing requires healthy connections between:
      • Self and Work: Finding purpose in productivity and mastery.
      • Self and Others: Strong, supportive human bonds.
      • Self and Something Larger: Coherence within a shared meaning system or truth.
  • The AI Threat: AI risks "cracking" these foundations by replacing human effort with shortcuts.
      • Meaningless Prosperity: Universal Basic Income (UBI) may provide money but guarantees "mass meaninglessness" if people lose the drive to be productive.
      • Social Erosion: Chatbots and AI companions may replace human intimacy, preventing the development of essential social and sexual relationship skills.
      • Fragmentation: AI-driven social media has already destroyed shared reality, creating a "fragmentation all the way down" where collective understanding is impossible.

The Crisis of Human Capital and Childhood

The transition to a "phone-based childhood" between 2010 and 2015 has led to a collapse in mental health and cognitive ability among Gen Z, a trend AI is poised to accelerate.

  • Developmental Destruction: Children need to do "hard things" thousands of times to build competence. AI shortcuts—like paper writing and book summarization—threaten to destroy future human flourishing.
  • The Critical Window: Haidt suggests we have roughly one year to raise consciousness and implement safeguards before AI integration becomes irreversible.
  • Accountability Gap: Tech companies currently operate with zero restraint or liability. Haidt calls for a "you break it, you fix it" regulatory approach, treating social media and AI like any other consumer product with a "death count."
  • Protecting Puberty: Brain development during puberty is the most fragile period; addictive algorithms and "engagement loops" have turned children into prisoners of their devices.

The Attention Crisis in Adults

While the focus is often on the "Anxious Generation" (Gen Z), Haidt notes that adults are suffering from a parallel cognitive decline.

  • Attention Fragmentation: The primary locus of damage is the disruption of deep work and cognition. Many adults now report an inability to read a full book or even a paragraph without distraction.
  • The "Stupidity" Paradox: As machines get smarter, humans are making themselves "stupider" by outsourcing their thinking and attention to mercenary platforms.
  • Public vs. Private Interaction: Moving from face-to-face, private communication to public, algorithmically mediated interactions has been a "disaster for human relationships."

Restoring Civil Discourse

To combat political polarization, Haidt advocates for moral psychology skills that allow people to communicate across tribal divides.

  • Constructive Dialogue: Through the Constructive Dialogue Institute (CDI), Haidt promotes interpersonal skills to navigate "political trench warfare."
  • Curiosity Over Charity: Approach disagreements with curiosity. It is not an act of kindness but a tool to become smarter and more effective.
  • The "Acknowledge" Strategy: To open a dialogue, start by acknowledging something the other side is correct about. This signals respect and shifts the interaction from "war" to conversation.

Actionable Strategies for Leaders and Individuals

Haidt emphasizes that "willpower is overmatched" by the behavioral design of modern apps; therefore, environmental change is necessary.

  • Digital Hygiene:
      • Device Audits: List the first five things you do upon waking and the last five before sleeping. If they involve a phone, you are ceding control of your day to the device.
      • App Location: Remove addictive or "slot machine" apps (Instagram, video games, investment sites) from the phone. Access them only via computer to restore intentionality.
  • Morning Rituals: Prioritize human or physical contact (touching a person, a pet, or making coffee) before touching a screen to set personal intentions for the day.
  • Environment Design: Following the "Grit" philosophy of Angela Duckworth, change your physical environment to change your mental habits.

***

Notable Quotes

"AI is saying, 'Hey, take a shortcut. Don't write that paper, don't read that book, I'll summarize it for you.' That is a recipe for the absolute destruction of human capital and the destruction of future human flourishing." — Jonathan Haidt
"Happiness... is actually from between. It's from getting the right relationships between yourself and your work, yourself and others, yourself and something larger than yourself." — Jonathan Haidt
"In the age of AI, as our machines are getting smarter than us very, very quickly, we're helping it along by making ourselves stupider. This does not bode well for the human future." — Jonathan Haidt
"Willpower is overmatched... the apps are designed by people who've studied behaviorism and gambling addiction. You have to do behavioral change: change your environment, and then you can change your thinking." — Jonathan Haidt

They were very anxious, depressed, with increasing rates of suicide and self-harm. So I've been studying that since 2015. And while there are many causes of it, I now believe that the biggest cause is the massive technological transformation that we all went through between, really, 2010 and 2015, when we moved everything onto smartphones and social media. That, I believe, is causing democratic dysfunction, it's causing mental health declines, especially among the young, and it's causing educational declines; test scores are dropping as well. So that's what I'm studying now: what is happening to us and what do we do about it? Very good. Well, and so with all of that, you know, we have this AI age where all of this new technology is freeing us from rote work and supposedly making it easier for us to do all the things that only humans can do, the things we can do best. In that world, do you think that we'll finally crack happiness? Crack it in the sense of destroy it, yes. I think if we don't take care, if we go on the path we're going on, it will be extremely hard for most people to have a flourishing life. So, here's what I mean by that. The causes of happiness, we know, come not from material prosperity, although that helps. It comes from getting the right relationships: healthy relationships between yourself and your work, yourself and other people—that is, strong supportive human relationships—and yourself and something larger than yourself, the sense that you're embedded in a meaning system in which you can find coherence and purpose. Those are the foundations of human happiness in any age. What is AI doing? It's going to make it extremely good for a few people who will be coordinating many AIs. We're going to have extraordinary income inequality as a few people use the technology. But since AI will soon be able to do almost everything better than humans, especially once we get it put in robots, it's going to be very hard, I think, in the future for people to find meaningful work. I'm especially fearful for young people, my children's generation, teenagers now. What entry-level jobs will there be for them in a few years when they're on the job market? AI is going to make it extremely difficult to have meaningful relationships. From now on, children will be growing up with chatbots as their best friends. They're not going to learn the skills of human interaction; those are already declining and AI will decline them much further. Young people will have sexual relationships with AIs. They will not learn how to have sexual relationships with humans. And ultimately I believe those relationships will be unsatisfying. And finally, your relationship to something larger than yourself. Social media has already ripped to shreds any shared sense of coherence, meaning, facts, and truth. Whatever happens, we will not be able to understand it collectively again. In the United States, there will be a left reading and a right reading, and never the twain shall meet, and even within that, it's fragmentation all the way down. So, I suspect that AI will crack happiness; it will break it so that it will be much harder for people to obtain. "Sure. Well, in your mind, what do people get wrong about happiness when we talk about this, that might even fuel this gap?" So, in my first book, *The Happiness Hypothesis*, I explored three hypotheses about happiness. The first, the most obvious, the most simple-minded is: happiness comes from getting what you want. And if we can just give people what they want—material prosperity, cars, good clothing, food, entertainment—they'll be happy. No. Entertainment and pleasure are valuable parts of life, but they do not bring happiness. We adapt to them very quickly and then we feel empty. One of my great fears about what people are thinking about AI is: "Oh, don't worry about it. There's going to be so much prosperity that we'll just give UBI, Universal Basic Income. We'll give everyone so much money." That guarantees mass meaninglessness. That means that our young men in particular are just going to be hooked up to porn and video games and whatever comes next. And if they are not able to be productive, to do something useful, they will be mired in the depression and sense of meaninglessness that they're already in. So, let's put that aside. Material prosperity is not going to make us happier; in fact, I believe it's going to now, for the first time, begin to make us less happy, although of course it has helped the increase until now. The second happiness hypothesis: happiness comes from within. This is what the ancients said, this is what the Buddhists said, this is what the Stoics said. And there's a lot of truth to that one; that one's a very good hypothesis psychologically. And that's true as far as it goes; people will need to work on themselves. But what I found in my research is that ultimately it's not even from within. It's actually from *between*. It's from getting the right relationships between yourself and your work, yourself and others, and yourself and something larger than yourself. And if AI and technology were to help those three relationships, then it would help us be happier. But I believe AI is going to block each of those three relationships, leaving each of us in our own individual pod consuming mass quantities of entertainment, which will be so much more gripping and beautiful than it is today, so people will never leave, and Aldous Huxley's vision in *Brave New World* will come true. "If we did everything right, right, and we'll get to some of the changes we can make, but if we did everything right, what would the world look like? How would it be different? A different version of the future?" So, if we were to do everything right, what would that look like? Well, first let's start with what needs to be done. So, there are several really basic things. The first and most basic is that these industries—social media is what I've focused on, I try not to say "tech" because tech does so many wonderful things, but social media and now AI, which is so much richer and more powerful than social media—and it's actually the same companies to some extent. Those companies have operated with no restraints. No legislation in the United States, nothing, zero, ever to restrict them. There is no other consumer product that is used by almost all children that already has a body count, a death count in the thousands at minimum, that is causing mass destruction of human capital. There's never been a consumer product in which there was no regulation, no lawsuits; they have never faced a jury, they have never lost a case against all these parents whose children are dead. So there is zero accountability for these companies that are transforming our society, hurting millions of people, and doing whatever they want. So that's the first thing: governments are going to have to say, "You know what? You break it, you fix it, you're responsible for the people you're damaging." That's the most basic thing, is accountability. Another is that we're going to have to protect childhood, especially through puberty. Right now, we let—what happened 10, 15 years ago—we let Silicon Valley run an experiment on our children. They said, "How about we give all our toddlers iPads so that they go through their early years swiping instead of interacting?" That's not working out well; kids have tremendous developmental delays, language delays, behavioral problems, and attentional problems. So we've already disrupted early childhood and mid-childhood. And then when I started this, it was teenagers who were on social media, but now it's 7 and 8-year-olds on TikTok. So now children go through puberty not going through the experiences that they need to develop themselves socially, sexually, and intellectually, but as prisoners to engagement loops and addictive algorithms. So we are completely messing up the most fragile and important periods of brain development. We did it with social media, it's been a complete disaster around the world, and we're about to do it again with AI, only it's going to be much more powerful and much faster. So we don't have 10 years, we have maybe one year. We've got to fix this, we've got to raise consciousness, we've got to understand. Yes, AI's gonna help adults do things more easily. But children have to do hard things, thousands of times. And AI is saying, "Hey, take a shortcut. Don't write that paper, don't read that book, I'll summarize it for you, I'll write it for you." That is a recipe for the absolute destruction of human capital and the destruction of future human flourishing. "And what impacts are these also having on adults, being able to focus, to pay attention, to do hard things? What effects will this have on adults?" When I was doing the research for *The Anxious Generation*, which I published in 2024, I was totally focused on young people. That's where we'll get the biggest bang for the buck, that's where the most damage is happening—we're changing brain development during puberty, we've got to stop that. And I didn't really look at adults because, A, we didn't have the evidence very clearly, and B, it's really hard to pass legislation to tell adults what to do. I don't want to do that. But boy, do we have to pass legislation to protect minors and children, okay. But since the book came out, wherever I go, wherever I speak, I'm talking about the children, and whatever adults I'm talking to, they say, "You know, it's happening to me too. I can't focus, I'm distracted, I'm more anxious when I'm on social media, it makes me self-conscious." So it's really clear the rewiring of social relationships to pull them away from direct one-to-one communication, private communication, a telephone call, face-to-face. When we've pushed all of that down and pushed it all onto public interactions mediated by companies that we know are mercenary, that we know hide the research they've done which finds harm, that has been a disaster for human relationships. And we're all feeling it. I now believe that while I focused on mental health as the primary locus of destruction from social media, I now believe that I got that wrong. That actually it's the attention fragmentation, it's the disruption of cognition and attention, it's the difficulty reading books. I can't read a book anymore; there's so much going on, so many things to click, I'm distracted. It's very hard for me to pay attention for a long time. And so many adults are having the same thing. There's even research showing that people are less able to read a paragraph—adults now—less able to read a paragraph and answer simple questions about it afterwards. We can't focus. So in the age of AI, as our machines are getting smarter than us very, very quickly, we're helping it along by making ourselves stupider. This does not bode well for the human future. "Some of these technologies that are driving fragmentation, they're also sort of driving these problems within the discourse, how we're able to relate to one another. You actually co-founded a thing called the Constructive Dialogue Institute in 2017. What is that and what kind of resources is it developing?" So, how does all of this relate to civil discourse, civil dialogue, and political polarization? My early work was on how our differences in morality, especially left versus right, are driving us to an increasingly polarized world in which we each have our own informational system, our own facts. And democratic politics, a liberal democracy, which is the greatest way to live together ever found by human beings—it's the achievement of the Enlightenment era to find ways of living together with diversity, with immigration, granting people the freedom to live the lives they want. This was the great flowering, especially in the second half of the 20th century. But what's happening now? Since social media took over in the early 2010s, what we're seeing is increasingly fragmented worlds of facts or belief, increasingly tribal politics—maybe not in every country, but certainly in the United States, we're seeing it in many European countries. We're seeing the left become increasingly extreme, focused on identity and identitarianism. We're seeing the right getting increasingly extreme, focusing again on identity, identitarianism: the people who were here 100 years ago are the "real" Americans, we're told. So this is a recipe for disaster in liberal societies, and this is what's happening in many places. So what I've been trying to do since 2016 and '17, since Donald Trump was elected and our splits became more magnified, is to use moral psychology to help people develop skills to talk across the divide and to understand people on the other side. This has been my mission since 2012 when I wrote a book called *The Righteous Mind*. The Constructive Dialogue Institute offers a program that you can use—it's ideally in classes in universities, but it'll work in high schools, it'll work in companies, it'll work in church congregations—that teaches people the basics of moral psychology: why do we hold the views we have? And it teaches them the skills of understanding: first listen, first ask questions, first understand which moral foundations they're using, why they feel that their view is morally imperative. Once you understand that, you can speak to them with respect, you can learn yourself, and you're better able to persuade them if you still want to persuade them. So the technology is amplifying our divisions and increasing rage, and what we're trying to do at CDI is teach interpersonal skills that will make young people better equipped to go out into the jungle—the political trench warfare that is American society and many other societies—and be prepared to navigate it. "People who are maybe experiencing some of the resources that you guys put together through CDI, what's maybe a habit or a change that they might make after kind of going through one of your programs? What's something that they would be doing?" Well, the first thing, if you're going to talk to someone—so let's suppose a lot of the times you have a disagreement with your own parents, that's what we often see, or your grandparents, or your cousins, or your friends. And we're seeing people nowadays, especially more on the left, cutting off people that they disagreed with. And this is a shame because, again, human relationships and close connections are the most important foundation of human happiness and flourishing. And as our technology is fragmenting us, we don't want to amplify that by having people cutting each other off. And so if you're having a conflict with someone, let's say you're on the left and they're on the right or vice-versa, the first thing you would want to do is learn about their view; you can either read about it or you can ask them. So start with curiosity. And if you start with curiosity, it's not charity—it's not that you're trying to be nice to them—ultimately it will make you smarter, better connected, and more effective. So start with curiosity, ask questions first. The next step, I believe, is acknowledge something that they're right about. They're always right about something. There's something you can say, "You know, you guys, your side, you really care about, you know, family or marriage or social stability, or you really care about inequality or the environment, and you're right. You know, you're right that those things are declining, we're in trouble." If you start by acknowledging that they're right about something, it works magic. It tells the person, "This is not a war. You're not just out to attack me and prove me wrong, you actually respect me." And that is the key; that opens their hearts and now you can actually talk. "And what about happiness in general? Are there things that you recommend, changes that people can have in their habits and their daily routines, that can maybe get them out of bad cycles?" So what can people do to be happier? I've taught a course actually since 2006 at the University of Virginia and since 2014 at New York University. I've taught a course called Flourishing. And I used to go through, and I still do, I go through the foundations, like let's get these things right. You've got to have exercise and movement; your body needs movement. I talk briefly about nutrition—not too much junk food and sugar. Sleep is an important foundation; if you're not sleeping seven hours a day. So there's all kinds of things that you need to do and that's been true for decades. All right. But what I've discovered in the last five years is that if we don't start with digital habits, then all is lost. Now this is especially true for the younger people, for my undergraduates, because so many of them are on social media 4 to 7 hours a day. 4 to 7 hours a day. Imagine you're a college student, you move to New York City to go to NYU, and what do you do? You go to classes and you sit on your bed and scroll. And that's it. What a waste of a college education, what a waste of your mind. And so what I've discovered is that anyone who is on social media for three or more hours a day, or who's on their phone for seven hours a day or more—and that's a lot of them—I won't let them work on anything else until they work on that. And the simplest trick: if you're on social media or video games or investing sites, anything that you go to habitually, anything that operates like a slot machine which gives you variable ratio reinforcement, get it off your phone. If you have to be on Instagram for some reason, do it only on your computer. It's when you have these addictive apps on your phone that the phone may move to the center of your life, take everything over, and at that point that's your biggest problem. If you don't work on that, I can't help you. Once I interviewed a man who changed the first thing he did in the morning, the first thing he touched. And so he stopped picking up his phone and he made a rule that the first thing he touched had to be another person. So he had to either pick up his baby or it had to be something alive; he had to touch his dog or he had to, you know, hug his wife or something. People don't even realize sometimes that they're reaching for the phone. "Is there a question they should ask themselves to maybe recognize that they're even doing it? So what habits can you develop with your phone to increase your chances of flourishing?" One thing that I've learned from teaching undergraduates and MBA students who are in their late 20s, is that almost all young people and a lot of older people, the very first thing they do when they wake up in the morning is check their phone for notifications. They don't get out of bed, they don't go to the bathroom, they don't make coffee; it's the phone. And what's the last thing they do before they close their eyes at night? Same thing, it's the phone. And what do they do in between? For a lot of them, mostly it's the phone. That is not a life. That is not a life with relationships. That means you are never fully present in human interactions. So one of the things that I strongly encourage all of my students to do and I encourage all adults to do: make a list of the first five things you do after you open your eyes. And look at it and say, "Is that really what I want?" and find ways to change it. Make a list of the last five things you do before you close your eyes. And look at it and say, "Is that really what I want?" And what everyone finds is when they do that, when they give themselves space to wake up and to set the day and to choose what they want to happen that day—set your intentions—you have a much better day. If you don't do that, you open your eyes, you look at your phone, your phone will now control your thinking, your cognition, your thoughts, your priorities, and your actions for the rest of the day. You'll constantly be interrupted, you'll never have deep work, and you won't really amount to anything. So willpower is overmatched; willpower is not enough. You can't just say, "I'm going to look at my phone last." You can't just say, "I'm going to be present in my social..." No, you cannot, that's not enough. The phones and the apps are designed by people who've studied behaviorism, they've studied gambling and gaming addiction, they used techniques from Las Vegas in designing these apps, and they are too strong for our willpower. As Angela Duckworth writes—she's the one who wrote the book *Grit*—you have to do behavioral change: change your environment, and then you can change your thinking and your mental habits. "And where can people go for more information?" The central hub is anxiousgeneration.com; that links out to all other sites. Also, if there are children in your life between 8 and 13 years old, we have a brand new book out called *The Amazing Generation*, which brings the kids on board; they're very excited to read it and to learn about what's happening to them. You can go to constructivedialogue.org for information on civil discourse programs, and you can go to letgrow.org for information about how to give your kids more freedom and independence in the real world. That is all the time we have today, John. Thank you so much, this was amazing. "My pleasure. I hope it's helpful. This is a global problem." Absolutely. And thanks so much for you, for listening and watching. Episode 52: Michael Paulus - Founder of PCM Encore

Generating Alpha Podcast(1.8K)·42:01·85 views·2026-05-04
Michael PaulusFounder, PCM Encore
TL;DRMichael Paulus, the serial entrepreneur who bootstrapped Assurance IQ to a $2.35B exit, is now scaling PCM Encore, a multi-family office that applies sophisticated "endowment-style" investment strategies and aggressive tax optimization to high-net-worth individual portfolios.

TL;DR

Michael Paulus, the serial entrepreneur who bootstrapped Assurance IQ to a $2.35B exit, is now scaling PCM Encore, a multi-family office that applies sophisticated "endowment-style" investment strategies and aggressive tax optimization to high-net-worth individual portfolios.

The Bootstrap Advantage: Building Assurance IQ

  • Strategic Bootstrapping: Paulus chose to bootstrap Assurance IQ—an insurtech platform—rather than raising venture capital because the life insurance market provided immediate upfront commissions that supported self-funding.
      • Operational Focus: By avoiding the "distractions" of VC networking and board meetings, the team could remain obsessively focused on customer journey and unit economics.
      • Hyper-Metric Tracking: Without a "safety net" of VC cash, the company tracked performance metrics down to the minute rather than the quarter.
  • The $2.35B Exit: The company sold to Prudential Financial in 2019 to solve for capital-intensive Medicare scaling and to gain control over the underwriting process via Prudential’s trillion-dollar balance sheet.

Transitioning from Venture Capital to Wealth Management

  • The a16z Experience: While serving as a Partner at Andreessen Horowitz, Paulus learned he was fundamentally an operator rather than an investor. He noted that great founders must have a "naive belief" in their success, whereas VCs often view opportunities too statistically.
  • The "Gilded Cage" Risk: Paulus warns that success often leads to a "gilded cage" where founders are surrounded by "yes-men"; he counters this by staying close to engineers and younger team members.
  • Genesis of PCM Encore: After managing his own wealth and his father-in-law’s (Logitech co-founder Daniel Borel), Paulus realized that individual investors lacked access to the sophisticated, tax-efficient "endowment-style" management used by institutions.

Investment Philosophy: The PCM Encore Strategy

  • Endowment-Style Portfolios: The firm focuses on 7–9 non-correlated asset classes to create resilience.
  • Tax Alpha: Unlike non-taxable endowments, PCM Encore prioritizes "after-tax" returns for individuals through:
      • Direct Indexing: Maximizing losses to offset gains.
      • Real Estate Depreciation: Utilizing 130–140% depreciation on every dollar invested in specific developments.
  • Real Estate Thesis: Paulus targets West Coast multifamily development (specifically in growth hubs like Bellevue) as a bond replacement.
      • Resilience: He views multifamily as counter-cyclical because people move back into apartments during recessions.
      • Rate Play: Underwriting deals at current interest rates creates "optionality"; if rates drop, mid-teens IRR deals can transform into 40–50% IRR "home runs."

The Future of Fiduciary Services and AI

  • AI as a Commodity Driver: Paulus believes AI will eventually commoditize basic financial planning and public market indexing, which is already a low-cost service.
  • The Human Edge: High-value wealth management will shift toward "uncommon investments" (private equity, off-market real estate) and hyper-complex estate planning—areas where AI lacks the necessary private data or human nuance.
  • Operational Efficiency: PCM Encore uses AI "under the hood" to increase team efficiency, but delivers the final service with a "human touch."

Career Wisdom for Young Entrepreneurs

  • Know Your DNA: Paulus emphasizes self-awareness—knowing if you are a risk-taking "dreamer" like a VC or a "risk manager" like a banker.
  • Avoid the Burnout Finish Line: He admits to facing mental health challenges in his 20s due to self-imposed pressure. His advice is to "enjoy the journey" rather than rushing toward a liquidity event that doesn't inherently bring happiness.
  • The Power of Mentorship: He credits much of his success to being mentored by Joe Lonsdale, who exposed him to unique, non-pedestrian business ideas early in his career at Addepar.
"The most important thing for me in my career is I never want to look at a clock... one of the best gifts, no matter what you do in your life, is that you're excited about the day." — Michael Paulus

This week on Generating Alpha, I sat down with Mike Paulus, Stanford-trained entrepreneur, former partner at Andreessen Horowitz, and founder and CEO of PCM Encore, a multi-billion dollar family office. Mike studied economics and management science and engineering at Stanford, where he helped manage a portion of the university's endowment through the student-run Blyth Fund. He went on to serve as president of Addepar, the wealth management technology platform that reports on trillions in assets, before co-founding Assurance IQ in 2016, an insurtech platform that matched customers with insurance products at scale, and sold to Prudential for $2.35 billion in 2019, making Mike a billionaire at exit as he bootstrapped the business. He's also spent time as a partner at Andreessen Horowitz and holds two patents for innovations in frontend technology. In 2021, he founded PCM Encore, a fiduciary, employee-owned multi-family office and has since grown it to billions of dollars in assets under management. In our conversation, we talk about his path from Stanford to Silicon Valley, building Addepar, co-founding and selling Assurance IQ, his time at a16z, why he started PCM Encore, how he thinks about alternatives and portfolio construction for high-net-worth families, and much more. If you enjoy this episode, please follow the podcast and rate it five stars on Spotify, subscribe on YouTube, and share it to anyone who you think might find it valuable. I really enjoyed speaking to Mike and I hope you guys enjoy the episode. Thank you. Thank you, Michael, for coming on the podcast. I really appreciate it. It's great to be here. Big fan. Thank you. Well, I want to start where I always do: the beginning, as you know. If I'm correct, you grew up on a blueberry farm in Washington State. Tell me about your childhood, the environment you grew up in, how that shaped you. I'd love to hear the backstory. Yeah, so grew up on a small blueberry farm almost on the Canadian border. And then my mom was the local Lutheran preacher. So it was a, it was a very idyllic childhood, but I was really always interested in the financial world and technology. And I think the farm taught me hard work, you know, certainly, but I also knew I wanted to get off the farm. You know, when you're going up and down the same row of blueberries for, you know, months on end, it gives you a little extra motivation to do well at school. My biggest memory was just thinking through the time till coffee time, then lunch, till the end of the day, just because the work was so monotonous. And I said, you know, the most important thing for me in my career is I never want to look at a clock. And I’ve been very fortunate to do things where you're excited about the day and, you know, that’s, that’s one of the best gifts, I think no matter what you do in your life, that you can take away. If I’m correct, after growing up there you go to Stanford and you earn two degrees, a bachelor's and a master's. What was that like, that contrast between growing up on a blueberry farm in Washington State and then going to Stanford, one of the most prestigious formal education institutions in the world? Tell me about your time there. What did you take away? What did you learn? And after that, how did you know what you wanted to do, or did you know what you wanted to do? Yeah, it's a great question. I think the biggest takeaway from Stanford from a positives standpoint was you get to meet your tribe. You get to meet people from all over the world that are like yourself. And I look at my best friends and they're from Nigeria. I met my wife at Stanford from Switzerland, and another good friend from Ireland. I think you meet a lot of folks there that are very different and that has its own benefits, but you also get to meet these people that have so much in common and it kind of distills that group from around the world. And then I think being there at such an interesting time—we had the 2000 crisis that Silicon Valley was just coming back from. You had Facebook really just starting to take off, you know, the coolest kids were getting internships there. And it was dynamic. I mean, Instagram was founded while I was there. Bobby Murphy from my freshman dorm was one of the Snapchat founders and that was happening there. So I think to be at that time in that place as Silicon Valley was really just reemerging, I think I was very fortunate. Was it during those college days when you knew you wanted to be an entrepreneur, or did it take some time after that to really understand that? You know, it’s interesting. I think I was ambitious and I didn't know how to channel that. I thought, you know, maybe I'll go into real estate development. I took a job at BCG, an internship at BCG, and I was going down an investment banking path. I’d seen the entrepreneurial and the tech wave as well. So I think like a lot of young people, I wanted to make something in my life. I wanted to work hard and get ahead and kind of have the American dream, but I don't think I was necessarily like the kids you see that drop out of freshman year and are just super entrepreneurial. I think later in my career I realized how much I loved working for myself, but that wasn't evident early on. I mean, I think my life could have very easily taken a different path. After Stanford, how did you channel that and ultimately end up at Addepar in 2009? What was the space in between that, or was it direct? Yeah, so it's a good story. I had a great internship at BCG. It only lasted a summer, but I was working for a bank, I was in the New York office and then they sent me to North Carolina to work for a bank. And it was great. It was a big IT revamp. That gave me my first view into just financial technology, understanding how backwards things were, and the core opportunity there. But you know, I had taken a job at Goldman Sachs in the TMT group. I've got a lot of good friends that have done that. It’s a great group with illustrious alumni, and that's what I thought I was going to do. And there was a person at Stanford named Steve Laughlin—or Steve Laughland rather, he’s actually a partner now at Accel, great founder. And then he said you should meet this guy, Joe Lonsdale. And Joe had been one of the Palantir co-founders, and I went to meet Joe and I'll never forget, it was a brand new Palantir building, no one had moved in, so it was an abandoned like four-story building in the middle of Palo Alto. I come into the office, Joe’s got a lightsaber collection behind him. I was like, am I even in the right place? And then as he started talking, you know, just an absolute brilliant, really a genius, but then it was so creative and we could go and do this and this and this. And there wasn't this thought of, hey, if you do this, you get this position to this position to this position. He really believed if you're young and smart and you work hard, like let’s go and do this. And it was just really invigorating and that really changed my life and caused me to go and build Addepar with him. During that time at Addepar, what were those things you pursued and worked on? What do you think you took away from that time there and from Joe? Yeah, so we had this vague idea that the financial crisis had happened, people had lost a lot of money, portfolios had not behaved like they were expected to, and how could we fix that? You know, kind of a typical Silicon Valley grandiose vision. And when we got there really we first channeled that into just meeting with hundreds of wealth managers. And that was just fascinating. Because of Joe and being in Silicon Valley, you got to meet with Iconiq as it was being founded, and IEQ, some of the best wealth managers across the country. So just to be a front-row seat to folks that are incredible at what they do. A lot of what came out of those conversations was we had evolved from this world of stocks and bonds to alternatives really—hedge funds, private equity, real estate, venture capital. And in a CAPM model, they all seem to help with portfolios’ diversification, but post-investment there wasn't really tracking that was happening. And if you look at the lead up to the financial crisis, ‘06 to ‘08, these were all going up together, which they shouldn't have done, right? And then it turned out that they all crashed together. So there was this real desire from the wealth management community, really anyone with a complex endowment-style portfolio, to better understand the risks they were taking, have a real understanding of the underlying exposures of the funds, and just wrap your head around this complexity. That was really what differentiated Addepar, and we had this incredible tailwind of the independent RIA movement as hundreds of billions or trillions of dollars moved out of wirehouses. So it was just very interesting, and it was fun to see so many incredible investors that have so many different strategies. I learned a lot about enterprise sales, you know, how to go make a $500,000 or million dollar sale as a 22-year-old and that process, implementation process, and scaling. It was fun. After Addepar you go to Andreessen, and you sit across the table from like, you were at Addepar and you were somewhat siloed to one company and then all of a sudden you’re a VC seeing all these founders. I guess my question around that is, now that you saw all those founders from that perspective, kind of what was it teach you about the difference between good and great founders, and how did you implement that into ultimately building Assurance IQ, which you did after? It's interesting. The first thing I’ll say is just getting to meet that team and Marc in particular, you see why he is such a singular person, how he invented the internet. I've just never seen someone that is a clear first-principle thinker, how he looks to the world and how he can live in the future. And then it was interesting because there’s other people like a Jeff Jordan, totally different, far more pragmatic, but incredible at marketplace businesses and a really good investor in his own right. And I think it's similar with founders. You sort of look at product, founder-market fit, and we called it the maze as well, what does a founder have that makes them unique? But, you know, you had the emergence of things like Bitcoin and Coinbase was an early investment at that point, and you kind of had to be a little bit crazy. And that was something that Andreessen really kind of embraced: what are the crazy sub-segments? What's going on? And there were a lot of misses there, but that’s also how you get the next big thing. Then there's founders that are just incredibly practical and detail-oriented and they find markets and businesses that are really effective for themselves or they're highly technical and they figure out things on the infrastructure side. So I think in a way, there’s not one prototype, but I think it's all about knowing what you're really good at and what you love and how does that match up to the business that you're taking? I think businesses have such a different DNA in terms of what's going to make you successful or not successful. So I’d say that and then I’d say I’m kind of happy as an entrepreneur I wasn't a VC first, because you see dozens and dozens of really smart teams attacking the same space. And I think you start to look at things more probabilistically or statistically. And I think as a founder, you just need that naive belief that you're going to make it happen. And you touched on that point of kind of each founder is different and has their own superpower in a sense. I'm interested in a lot of people found and then just sit on the VC side and stay there forever. You didn't, you moved on, you founded Assurance IQ. So why were you right for that business? What do you think are your unique abilities, and why did that work well when ultimately starting Assurance IQ? Tell me a bit about that kind of beginning. Yeah, it’s a great question. So you asked about being an entrepreneur, I think one thing I learned at Andreessen is I am someone that loves to steer my own ship. And even if I have the best boss in the world, which I did at Andreessen, I just chafed under it. I think I took a personality test years later. They were like, sort of authority and self-control, you’re sort of off the charts. So on one piece, I’m just sort of incapable of being an employee. I’m not sure if that’s a good or a bad thing, but it's me, so you’ve got to kind of embrace it. I found I love operating. I love being with a team every day in the day in and day out. So the cadence of being a great board member, it just wasn't for me. And I also found it's interesting: while I’ve been in technology, I have a more conservative view of the world. If you think about technology for wealth managers or insurance doing life and health insurance, these were pragmatic things. So I wouldn't be a good blockchain founder. I’m someone that has a DNA where I can really execute and synthesize and build the right product for a customer. I looked at the businesses that Marc Andreessen thought were great businesses, and when I was sitting there at the table, the ones that I thought were great—and there wasn't a lot of overlap. He was looking for grand slams. And for me there was, you know, where is there a practical problem where you can go where you can differentiate yourself? So I think for me it was, I needed to work for myself, I loved being an operator, just that team environment is just on a day-to-day basis I love. I just wasn't ready to sit in the investor chair and say no to 15 people a day as your typical day. And then, there's a way that, like, I’m not necessarily a moonshot investor, I’m a little too skeptical. So, I found markets that really suited myself. So, at Assurance really the core insight was, I think two things really: consumers were looking online for insurance and there wasn't really a great way for that to occur, and then when you were getting life insurance you had to go to the doctor, you had to get blood drawn, it was just a terrible process. So really the confluence for us was how could we use data to potentially streamline that process for consumers and then how could we take this digital consumer and give them an optimal journey? And in some cases that meant buying totally online, and in some cases it was how do you transition that person from online research and quoting potentially to an agent to help them through that process? We called it the art of the human touch. And after Andreessen you started Assurance in 2016. Very rarely do you see a company that exits for a billion dollars that doesn't raise venture money, and you guys didn't, you completely bootstrapped. So, I’m interested to hear how you started, why make the decision to become bootstrapped, and the entire evolution of Assurance until you ultimately exited it. Yeah, it's a great question. So I think the first thing with bootstrapping is you have to be in the right market from a cash flow perspective. So Addepar has raised hundreds of millions of dollars, it will be worth billions of dollars, it’s going to be a great outcome for employees and investors. And when you're building a SaaS business, you don't really have much choice, right? You're investing a lot upfront for hopefully a customer that's very sticky. We had to build a huge amount of infrastructure. So when we look at Assurance, I think the first piece is the structure of life insurance is remarkable, right? You get upfront commissions for virtually the entire value from the carrier. So in a way we said, what would be the reason to raise? If you have good economics and you're profitable, you should be able to bootstrap this and if you can't make it work there's probably something at the core of your business that's wrong. And I think we saw that with a lot of insurtechs. There was this thought of the economics will improve as I scale. No, like Google just gets more expensive, all of that stuff just gets worse. So I think we figured we had to get the core economics right and the cash flow followed just because of the nature of that business. I think as well we really did not want distractions, and I think moving from the middle of Silicon Valley where it was all sort of lunches and networking up to Bellevue, Washington, where we could just focus on our customers and really be out of sight, out of mind was so good because it was 10, 12 hours a day just totally focused on your customer. I think that was one of our biggest competitive advantages: we could just be obsessed with our customers, period. We weren't thinking about how a VC would see us in the next round, and we could do the right thing. Like if customers did better with an agent, we put them on an agent. We didn't have to make this story for a VC around how we were replacing agents. So you could really just practically follow what was right for the business and the customer rather than kind of the next narrative for VC. So I think that’s the biggest benefit you get from bootstrapping is just that customer focus and it's maniacal, right? If you could go bankrupt this next month, you are really focused on the customer. Everyone says they're focused on the customer, but it really sharpens it when you’ve got that pressure. And what does that environment feel like? What does the culture feel like when you're bootstrapping and there’s a chance you can go bankrupt every month? Because obviously you were in Addepar, which raised money, you were at Andreessen where you saw a lot of companies that raised money. How did you see that kind of differentiation in the cultures between a bootstrapped startup and one that has that safety net, that buffer? So, you know, first of all most small businesses in America are bootstrapped. So I think you get the appreciation of what most entrepreneurs are going through. We’re so lucky, the privileged few that can go out and raise a lot of money and have that buffer. I think for us if you marry bootstrap with growth, I think that's where it gets challenging. And a lot of it was every day you had to be so on top of your metrics. You know, so many, I think at Addepar we were so focused every quarter on what customers we were closing in the quarter. What did that look like? If you're going to spend $100,000 on Google this month and if you don't get that money back, the business is done. You're really focused on what did we do today, this morning, this minute? So I think probably the way we were able to grow really quickly to hundreds of millions of dollars of revenue while being bootstrapped was to take that period of reflection and analysis down to the day, the hour, the minute. There was no such thing as a quarter for us. Before we go back to the episode, I want to take a short break to talk about my sponsor, Rho. Thank you, and back to the episode. And after that evolution four years later, you ultimately exit to Prudential for 2.35 billion. How did you know it was the right time to exit? How does an exit of that magnitude come together? So for us we had really two things coming together. The first is the Medicare business was growing and becoming very significant for us. And Medicare from a cash flow perspective is the opposite of life insurance. You spend a lot to acquire a customer, that customer stays with you for a long time, it's illegal for carriers to advance you commission, so you're paid out over years. So, and we saw that was the opportunity, was growing very quickly, it was a great space, we had a competitive advantage. And quickly, a couple years after the acquisition, we had hundreds of millions of dollars of debt financing. So that was one side of it. The other was as an insurance carrier, if you think about the direct guys, the guys who do a lot of advertising, they are all also the carrier and the underwriter because there's this tension, right? You're spending a lot of money to acquire someone so you want an underwriting process that's going to approve most people. That’s why the Geicos, the Progressives, they do both and they usually have something for almost anyone. If you look at the brokerage market, it's a little bit different. You have a panel of carriers and then each individual carrier can be a little bit pickier about their risk. We saw if we wanted to continue to innovate in the product and then critically have control over that underwriting—because you know, a small change in the Swiss Re algorithm and our economics would be underwater. So we saw that there needed to be a synergy there given the hundreds of millions of dollars of life insurance that we were writing. That scale means basically a trillion dollar balance sheet, and that really led to the process of us becoming part of Prudential. After that exit, you built what you're kind of working on now, or at least the first instance of it, which was PCM Encore, initially as a family office and then expanding it to become a multi-family office. Why wealth management? Why build in wealth management? Yeah, so it's been very organic. After I sold and was really kind of out, I went to play golf for a couple of weeks and I was miserable, I’m terrible at golf. But also I just love being around really smart people. I love building things. So I think the first permutation was really for myself and my family. How do we optimize this portfolio and then how do I work with great entrepreneurs? I love that, I love investing with entrepreneurs, I love being very involved, I like rolling up my sleeves. Then, I kind of alluded to this I think before we were on camera, but my father-in-law, was the co-founder of Logitech, said, hey, would you think about managing my money? There really is no upside in managing your father-in-law's money, it's just awkward Thanksgivings if you mess up. But we did and it was great. It worked out great and he had a totally different set of objectives, a different tax situation. So the portfolio looked very different from my own. And I loved that. That really got me thinking about the next evolution of this and led us in 2024 to become a multi-family office and open to others. I think if I look at that motivation coming back to what I love to do every day, which is work with really, really smart people and be part of that team. Even as a billion dollar plus single-family office, you really can't get the best talent. It's sub-scale, you know, there's not necessarily opportunity. I saw that a lot at Addepar, these small single-family offices that were just a bit sleepy, and that’s not the environment that I love. Part of growth is let's go get the best tax expert that we can, let's go get the best investment team, let’s scale out the private market side. So that’s a lot of how I looked at it is really even for myself and my family, how do we have one, just the broadest set of services and two, how do I surround myself with really smart people every day. And from a high level, what is PCM Encore now, and what's the investment philosophy? How is it differentiated than your average multi-family office out there? Yeah, so if we start at the top, we're an employee-owned fiduciary, and we have a wholly owned tax practice. So we’ll come to a client and we’ll say look, there’s going to be some part of it that we’ll manage, but we want to understand everything. Often the best opportunities are in making sure you have full interest deductibility on your mortgage, how do you structure your private investments, what are you doing in retirement areas. Oftentimes it’s really not the money that we’ll actually manage where there's the biggest tax advantages. So it’s very holistic. Underneath that, we really look at endowment-style portfolios and our view is, what are the best family offices and endowments and foundations doing? They have found seven to nine asset classes that have really independently or somewhat independently non-correlated returns and you find by putting a portfolio together there you have better outcomes, more return, and less risk. You’re able to create a lot of resilience. I think a lot of what we're looking to do is bring that style of portfolio to the individual investor. And the biggest difference, right, is that you and I pay taxes. And if you’re in the state of New York or California, that can be 45 or 50 percent. So a typical endowment-style portfolio is pretty darn inefficient for a taxable investor. You're mostly throwing off gains where through direct indexing and things like real estate and real assets and depreciation, we’re always thinking how do we maximize losses that this client can use either within the portfolio or elsewhere in their life. And then underneath that, we say where can we find uncommon investment opportunities? That tends to be in private markets, in real estate. We have really a lower middle market private equity strategy that we like and then we’re very involved in real estate. So we pick the couple of places where we want to play and really differentiate returns and then outside of that it's either where are these commoditized parts of the portfolio where we’re very fee-focused, and where do we partner with exceptional managers that we really think are worth it? I want to touch on real estate a tad bit. If I’m correct, you’ve deployed over like 300 million at this point into real estate. How do you think about it as an asset class within the broader portfolio, and then how do you think you also approach it uniquely? Because if I’m correct you have like an on-the-ground team in real estate. Yeah, and it’s interesting. I probably don't like most things in real estate. I’d say, you know, 95% of real estate deals I wouldn't touch. As you look at real estate there's a lot of different components to it. As you look at hospitality or office, these are interesting and they can have great returns, but I look at that as taking equity-like risk. You're correlated with credit cycles, you’re correlated with economic cycles, and oftentimes I’ll say for that part of the portfolio, if we're going to take those risks within a private equity or even just a simple equity portfolio, we’re probably going to outreturn things on an after-tax basis. The area that I really focus is where can real estate be resilient? I don't want to say I hate bonds, but you’re often basically going backwards, especially on an after-tax basis, and almost certainly your real earnings power has been deflated. If you owned a bond since 2020 you've lost 25 or 30% of that just to inflation. So I look at where can we find real estate that has that bond component to a portfolio? It’s going to be acyclical or counter-cyclical, you have a strong cash flow component, and it creates resilience. The area I’ve really focused on is multifamily, and in particular up and down the West Coast. I like it because in recessions, people are forced back into apartments. It’s inflation-resistant. In fact, rents are the single biggest component to inflation, so you get that. With the new big beautiful bill, we’re seeing depreciation of 130 to 140% of every dollar invested, which is pretty remarkable. And you put structures like Opportunity Zones and things on top of that, or being a qualified real estate professional and it’s a pretty remarkable thing for the taxable investor to look at. So now we’ve got an asset class where we can comfortably expect low teens returns. If we go into development, which is what we do, those can be well into the 20s, but it's counter-cyclical or acyclical, it’s inflation-resistant, it often behaves unrelated to the stock market or potentially sometimes in the opposite direction. So now I’ve got something where if I compare it to a bond, it’s looking really attractive. And how can you use things like a private real estate infrastructure to sort of replace that component of your portfolio and get really equity-like returns? On an after-tax basis, you know, probably outperform what the stock market should be expected, but with something that has genuinely less correlation and resilience. From a broad macro perspective, where are the opportunities now? What are you excited about, kind of what excites you and what kind of inefficiencies you see in certain markets? So, I think there's a couple of things that are happening that are exciting to us. The first, my favorite word is kurtosis. And I think when something really extraordinary happens, are you in a position to benefit? When we founded companies, that was the best example of that. I think if you talk to a lot of great asset managers or even myself, like when we started Assurance, we weren't thinking, hey, let’s go build a multi-billion dollar business that we’re going to bootstrap, but we gave ourselves that opportunity, right? I think what you have right now in real estate is underwriting deals at today's interest rates. If you have something that pencils to a mid-teens or low 20s return, and you're not trending rents or being aggressive, I think it's unlikely you’re going to see interest rates move against us. I think your worst-case scenario is they probably stay about where they are. And if you have a deal that works there, now we have this potential with a new Fed chair and policy. Let's just say you see 100 basis point reduction, which I think is very feasible. All of a sudden you have cap rates that are 20% lower. Now all of a sudden these are 40 or 50% IRR deals. So I think what's really interesting right now from a macro perspective is it's a really interesting way to play rates, assuming that's not baked in, assuming the deal works standalone. The accelerated depreciation last year is just enormous, and you know, we’re seeing Opportunity Zone investments where you can get an IRR in the 20s or 30s just from the tax benefits, from the deferral, the depreciation and no recapture. It’s pretty remarkable. Then you had a lot of inventory and a lot of starts during COVID, during this zero interest rate environment, and then really nothing has started in the past three to four years. So what we're seeing is this supply cliff that’s really starting now as the very last COVID inventories are coming on. I think the key is you’ve got to be in the right cities. If you’re in a growing city, you’re in an attractive place to be, you’re in a place where people appreciate new product, you can really benefit on the rental side too. We’re starting to see double-digit rent increases in the Bay Area, which we sort of see as the canary in the coal mine. Our view is that’s going to shift up to other growing kind of tech-forward markets. Bellevue is one that we like particularly. So that’s a lot of the thesis and the thing I like about it is it's kind of like an option. If none of the things I tell you are true, we’re still going to be fine. You’re still going to have a great return and you're going to get all the tax treatment, but I think whereas five years ago the interest rates and everything were really priced to perfection—hard to see the upside. Here there’s a lot of things that if they break your way, it takes a solid investment and makes it a home run. On the topic of opportunities, I want to take a second to touch on AI. I actually have a friend who's recently sold a fintech company that is now trying to build an AI-first multi-family office. In terms of how you look at AI both from a perspective of building the wealth management firm but then also allocating capital, what are some of the most unique insights you have that you think are valuable to share? Well, so I think the first piece is on the investment side. You were able to buy a low-cost index fund for the past three decades and outperform basically any active manager without a wealth manager present. And the wealth management industry has boomed during that period. So I think you first have to say just the ability to create a diversified portfolio is, especially in the public markets, somewhat commoditized and has been for a while. So there is something about that human touch. We use a lot of AI in efficiency but behind the scenes. I think that comes down to how can you do more for clients? I think there’s going to be a segment of the market that likes doing things themselves, that are very fee sensitive, and I think that group is already talking with AI models and doing that sort of thing. I do think AI presents an opportunity for wealth managers to do a far better job. As I look at what we do, looking at everything in your life and those opportunities, that's not what's happening in the industry. Most folks are like, wire me in your cash and I’ll manage this part of the portfolio. I hope that it can really make the average wealth manager significantly better. As I look at it from an investment standpoint, you can kind of be bifurcated. You can either not be in AI at all. So, people are going to need apartments no matter what happens with AI. Or a pest control company—I think that's going to be the last thing that a machine is going to do. We’ve got a lot of franchise restaurants. I think that's going to be something that's very resilient. And if you just don't want to deal with it, like go play there. Or you go into these industries where you’re right in the middle of it, right? And I think that’s the most exciting place to be. That’s where the value is being created. That’s where things are getting changed up. But you better be ready to dance if you’re going to do it. I look at us and I think absolutely our business needs to transform, in how the value that you give to clients and how you grow. I think you could see fee compression. I think you could see the economics change. So I think it's a really—but those are the dynamic right places to be. My thesis tends to be a ton of technology under the hood delivered with human touch and expertise. And then I also think that uncommon investment side is going to become more and more important. What are you sourcing that’s unique? I think for a lot of reasons that’s going to be maybe the last thing the AI can do, mostly because the best data on uncommon investments is not anywhere on the internet. There's no model for it to pick up. As I think about our business, the ability to give really unique opportunities for our clients is important. And then just the most complex situations, right? Really complicated estate structures, sophisticated interactions. I think the basic financial plan that you're using a piece of software for already will increasingly become commoditized. And I’m hopeful. Most consumers make terrible financial decisions. So I’m also hopeful that for me there’s a lot of mission here. Most Americans are not able to qualify for our services. If ChatGPT can do a better job than Reddit on giving you financial advice, hopefully that benefits everyone. With AI, the flow of information is much faster and much more efficient and thus you also have the problem of separating signal from the noise. So to ask you a question tactically, what does a day in the life of Michael Paulus look like in the sense of what do you read, what information do you consume, and how do you separate that signal from that noise? Yeah, it’s a good question. I’m a Wall Street Journal and FT guy in the morning and I like that European perspective. Then, I always like a good podcast. I like yours. A risk as you have success in life is you kind of build a gilded cage for yourself and everyone kind of says yes and you sort of create these layers between you and the team. That’s also why I love to be on client calls. I think I learn a lot. These are all very successful people in their own way and I try to spend a lot of time on that. Anthropic's Boris Cherny: Why Coding Is Solved, and What Comes Next

Sequoia Capital(175.0K)·24:36·99.3K views·2026-05-04
Boris ChernyCreator of Claude Code, Anthropic Lauren ReederPartner, Sequoia Capital
TL;DRCoding is effectively "solved" for standard distributions, shifting the developer's role from writing syntax to managing autonomous agent loops.
Boris Cherny predicts a democratization of software development on par with the invention of the printing press, where domain...

TL;DR

Coding is effectively "solved" for standard distributions, shifting the developer's role from writing syntax to managing autonomous agent loops. Boris Cherny predicts a democratization of software development on par with the invention of the printing press, where domain expertise replaces technical skill as the primary barrier to entry.

The Evolution of Claude Code

  • From Autocomplete to Agents: Claude Code was built to address "product overhang," where model capabilities exceeded available tools. It transitioned from simple "type-ahead" (tab-complete) functionality to an agentic model that writes 100% of the code.
  • Model-Driven PMF: Product-market fit was not immediate; adoption inflected only as model intelligence reached specific thresholds, starting with Opus 4 in May 2025 and accelerating through version 4.7.
  • Language Distribution: The model is most effective with "on-distribution" languages like TypeScript and React. While it can now learn new frameworks, Cherny highlights that choosing common languages allowed the model to take over 100% of the codebase earlier.

The Future of the Developer Workflow

  • Mobile-First Engineering: Cherny’s current workflow is largely phone-based, managing dozens of sessions and hundreds of active agents simultaneously via the Claude app.
  • The Power of Loops: The "slash loop" command is identified as the most significant workflow shift.
      • Automated Maintenance: Loops use cron jobs to schedule recurring tasks like fixing CI flakiness, auto-rebasing PRs, and clustering user feedback from social media.
      • Server-Side Routines: These allow tasks to persist and execute even when the developer’s laptop is closed.
  • Extreme Productivity: Using these autonomous agents, Cherny reports shipping up to 150 PRs in a single day, a feat impossible for manual coding.

Organizational and Market Disruptions

  • The Rise of the Cross-Disciplinary Generalist: Teams are shifting toward "generalists" where every role (Product Managers, Designers, Finance, Research) writes code. Specialist domain knowledge is becoming more valuable than the ability to write syntax.
  • Eroding Business Moats:
      • Decreased Moat Value: "Switching costs" and "process power" (proprietary workflows) are losing value because AI can easily port data and hill-climb complex processes.
      • Sustained Moat Value: Network effects, scale economies, and cornered resources remain critical defenses.
  • Startup Advantage: Cherny predicts a 10x increase in startups disrupting incumbents. Small teams can now compete head-to-head with large companies because they lack the "internal resistance" to retraining and evolving legacy business processes.

Democratization and The "Printing Press" Analogy

  • Mass Literacy for Software: Cherny compares the current AI inflection to the 15th-century printing press. Just as the press moved European literacy from 10% to 70%, AI will make software creation a basic life skill similar to sending a text message.
  • End of the Manual Era: Anthropic internally claims to have "no more manually written code." All SQL, communications, and scripts are generated by models, with agents communicating with other agents via Slack to resolve technical unknowns.
"For me today, you know, like the model writes 100% of my code. I write somewhere, you know, usually a few dozen PRs every day. Uh, there was a day last week I did like 150 PRs in a day. That was like, that was a record." — Boris Cherny
"Software will be a thing that is fully democratized, that anyone can do... The best person to write accounting software, I think maybe even today, is not an engineer, it's a really good accountant. Because they know the domain really well and coding is the easy part." — Boris Cherny
"We have no more manually written code anywhere at the company. All of the SQL is written by models. Everything is just built by the models." — Boris Cherny
