Howard Marks: AI, Debt vs Equity & The Next 40 Years Of Investing | Nikhil Kamath | People by WTF

Nikhil Kamath(2.2M)·103:17·55.1K views·2026-05-04
Howard MarksCo-founder, Oaktree Capital Management Nikhil KamathHost and Co-founder, Zerodha
TL;DRTrue investment outperformance requires "second-level thinking" that departs from consensus, as market cycles are driven by emotional excesses rather than predictable mechanical laws.
While artificial intelligence can optimize pattern recognition, exceptional human...

TL;DR

True investment outperformance requires "second-level thinking" that departs from consensus, as market cycles are driven by emotional excesses rather than predictable mechanical laws. While artificial intelligence can optimize pattern recognition, exceptional human insight remains essential for navigating unpredictable market shifts, pricing distressed assets, and managing downside risk.

The Philosophy of Preparation and the Mechanics of Cycles

  • The Concept of Mujo: Howard Marks attributes much of his foundational perspective to his study of Japanese literature, specifically the concept of *Mujo*—the inevitability of change.
      • Inherent Unpredictability: Because change is inevitable, unpredictable, and uncontrollable, investors must learn to accommodate to it rather than trying to forecast specific outcomes.
  • Preparation vs. Prediction: Marks emphasizes that investors should focus on preparation rather than prediction.
      • Portfolio Sub-optimization: Rather than building a portfolio optimized for a single predicted future, investors should build portfolios that can survive multiple potential futures, avoiding extreme vulnerability to negative tail events.
  • The Psychology of Market Cycles: Economic cycles do not behave like regular physical waves; they are dictated by human behavior.
      • Excesses and Corrections: Market cycles oscillate around a long-term trend line due to psychological extremes—excessive optimism leads to over-expansion, which inevitably requires a correction down to the mean.
      • The Illusion of the Average: While the S&P 500 has returned an average of 10% annually over the last 100 years, its actual annual return is almost never between 8% and 12%. The market is almost always reacting to excesses of greed or fear.

Debt Investing and Oaktree’s Risk-Controlled Strategy

  • The Appeal of Contractual Returns: Despite having capped upside compared to equity, debt investing appealed to Marks's naturally conservative disposition.
      • Underpriced Assets: Oaktree historically targeted fields that the broader market viewed with extreme skepticism, such as high-yield "junk" bonds or distressed debt (rebranded as "opportunistic credit").
  • Calculating the Probability of Default: The core skill set at Oaktree is predicting default probabilities more accurately than the consensus.
      • Excess Returns: If the market demands a high interest rate based on an assumed 5% default rate, but Oaktree's bottom-up analysis determines the true risk is only 2%, they capture an "excess return."
  • Institutional Discipline: Oaktree has maintained a default rate roughly one-third of the market's 3.6% to 3.7% long-term high-yield average through specific structural choices:
      • Lifetime Analysts: Unlike firms where analysts quickly transition to portfolio managers, Oaktree encourages analysts to remain in their roles long-term, building deep institutional knowledge.
      • Rigorous Coverage: Analysts must evaluate eight core areas of inquiry for every company to ensure they do not miss systemic blind spots.

The Limits of AI and the Power of "Second-Level Thinking"

  • AI's Edge and Boundaries: AI models excel at analyzing historical data, identifying repeating patterns, and executing strategies with disciplined consistency, free from fear and greed.
      • The Novelty Problem: AI is structurally limited when dealing with situations that have no historical precedent or pattern, such as evaluating unique business plans or identifying exceptional leaders like Steve Jobs.
  • Second-Level Thinking: To achieve above-average investment performance, an investor must think differently and *better* than the consensus.
      • The Herd Limitation: If an investor relies on the same consensus data and thinking as everyone else, they will achieve average results.
      • Uncoachable Trait: Just as you "cannot coach height" in basketball, Marks believes you cannot easily teach the intuitive, non-consensus mindset required for second-level thinking.
  • The Valuation of Information: Marks notes that readily available quantitative data about the present cannot yield superior returns because it is already priced into the market. Superior insight relies on interpreting this data differently.

Mindset, Intentionality, and Lifelong Learning

  • Drifting vs. Intentionality: Marks describes the first 35 years of his career as being "adrift" on a river of happenstance and luck, which included his transition from equities to bonds in 1978.
      • The Entrepreneurial Trigger: It was not until the founding of Oaktree in 1995 that Marks transitioned to acting with deliberate, proactive intentionality. He suggests that entrepreneurship naturally forces individuals to stop drifting.
  • Staying Sharp at Eighty: Marks maintains his cognitive agility through genetic luck, puzzle-solving, reading outside his field, and collaborating with younger professionals—specifically his son, Andrew.
      • Intellectual Flexibility: Despite working in credit for nearly five decades, Marks remains willing to challenge his own assumptions, as demonstrated by his recent deep dives into generative AI models.

Notable Quotes

"You can't predict, you can prepare. We can't predict the future, we can't control the future, but we can prepare for the future." — Howard Marks
"Just remember, none of this is easy. Anybody who thinks it's easy is stupid." — Charlie Munger (quoted by Howard Marks)
"If you think the same as everybody else, you'll act the same as everybody else. If you act the same as everybody else, your investments will perform the same as everybody else. So to perform better you have to deviate from the herd." — Howard Marks
So I'll give you a little bit of a background. Most of these are like conversations—I don't use the word "interviews" for what we do. Our conversations are fairly free-flowing. The audience we cater to are entrepreneurs of Indian origin who are around the age of 25 to 30. Everyone's looking to invest in the market, trade the market, start a business, or build something from the bottom up. So we're speaking to them, and most of today is around that. Can we start with a little bit about you and how your own journey began from Queens to Wall Street? How do you describe your story in your own words—your story, your origin story? Well, you know, I was born in Queens, New York, as you know—one of what are called the outer boroughs. I had a middle-class upbringing; neither parent went to college. But my father was a very intelligent man and an accountant. I think he did his job well, and so we lived a comfortable middle-class existence. I went to the public schools of New York at a time when you could get a good education in the public schools of New York, and I think I got one. Oddly enough, I ended up taking courses in business law and then accounting in high school. I really connected with accounting, with the orderliness and the symmetry. It just clicked for me. So I decided to go to business school. I applied to Wharton as the best business school for undergraduates in America. I was told I wouldn't get in, but I did. I went to Wharton as an accounting student and then switched my major to finance. Then I went on to get an MBA in accounting from the University of Chicago Business School. Between the years of business school, I had a job in the investment research department of Citibank. I liked it and went back. That's my background. Is Wharton, would you say, still one of the better schools? No, I think... I have no reason to believe it's not still the best undergraduate business school. There are other good graduate schools that are very competitive with Wharton. I'm no expert—I'm going to say that a hundred times on this tape—but I believe that Wharton is still the best undergraduate business school. And you studied Japanese literature at Wharton? Yes. When I went to Wharton, there were two very enlightened requirements. You had to have a semester of the literature of a foreign country, and you had to have a non-business concentration, what we call a minor. You know, the wonks would take statistics, or economics, or political science. Sorry, what are the wonks? Wonks? Yeah. Oh, you know, people who just think about making money or just think about numbers and business. But for my literature requirement, for some reason I don't remember, I took Japanese studies—Japanese literature. I think perhaps it was the exoticism, and I just fell in love with it. So I ended up taking a second semester of Japanese literature, then two semesters of Japanese civilization, and then one semester of Japanese art. And what did that teach you? Well, it taught me a lot, but the main things I've carried away have been some aspects of the Japanese philosophy. The most important one, the most relevant one, is something called *Mujo*, which literally means the turning of the wheel of the law. What it means in everyday life is the inevitability of change—that change is inevitable, unpredictable, and uncontrollable. We have to accommodate to it and make the most of it rather than think we can control it. Should one expect it? One should; it's inevitable. Yes, of course one should expect change. One should not expect a specific change, because it's unpredictable. I once read somewhere that a Western man, if it rains for five days, will expect it to rain on the sixth. Whereas an East Asian person, say Japanese for example, if it rains for five days will expect no rain on the sixth, because the pendulum, like you say, swings back to the mean. Is that culturally true? I have no reason... no, I don't know. But what I would say is, if the probability of rain is, let's say, 50-50, some people think five days of rain means the sixth day will bring rain because of a continuation of the trend. Others think five days of rain means it won't rain the next day because the rain has all been used up. What I think is, on the sixth day, it's 50-50. The previous days do not matter for the sixth day. Well, in weather that may not be precisely true, but I'm a big believer in the independence of events. I think that's what *Mujo* is about: the independence of events. If you flip a coin ten times—which is a better example than rain, because with rain there are physical properties at work—and you get ten heads, the probability of heads on the eleventh flip is still 50-50, because the trials are independent. Do you believe in game theory? In game theory? Well, I believe that you can strategize. You can figure out which action has the highest expected payoff, or the highest maximum potential payoff, or the least probability of a really bad payoff. You can perhaps even strategize about what your opponent is going to do, so I believe in strategy. I don't know if that's exactly the same as game theory, but I think that's how it works. As I've suggested in my comments so far, what I really don't believe in is predictions. I believe that given human nature and our dislike for ambiguity, people pursue predictions so they can understand what world they're going to be operating in and deal with it accordingly. If they do this to excess, then they theorize about a world they'll be operating in which is not as likely to occur as they think, which means their predictions can be misleading. About twenty-odd years ago, I wrote a memo called "You Can't Predict, You Can Prepare." That is really the essence. You can tell that comes directly from the understanding of *Mujo*. We can't predict the future, and we can't control the future, but we can prepare for the future. Now, that raises an interesting contradiction in terms: if you can't predict what the future will be, how can you prepare for it? The answer is that you can prepare for a future which is unpredictable. In investing, rather than putting together a portfolio that will produce maximum success if one specific future unfolds, you can put together a portfolio which will do fine if any of several futures unfold, and not too terribly if a bunch of other futures unfold. Of course, you can't prepare simultaneously for the positive tail and the negative tail. If you prepare for a total disaster, then you're unprepared for a great future. If you prepare for enormous success to maximize your outcome should that success unfold, then you're probably totally unprepared for a very negative outcome. But you can prepare if you sub-optimize for a number of possible outcomes in the middle of the probability distribution. Isn't that a bit contrarian to the coin example? Is it? If you toss a coin and it comes up heads ten times, and I toss it the eleventh time, you still expect the odds to be 50-50. So I can't look at past data to predict the future or prepare for the future. Then how do I prepare? I think the difference is that coin tosses are independent. The result of the preceding coin tosses has no impact on the next one. That's not true in the areas I work in. Most importantly, history, the things that have happened so far, and people's reaction to history have semi-predictable implications for the future. Not history, but people's reaction to it? Well, not history itself as much. History is the history of what people did, and what people did in the past, by itself, has implications for the things that will unfold tomorrow, next month, and next year. So in a way, are you saying the psychology of people—mass mentality—remains the same and that can be modeled more so than events can be? Well, of course, events are largely the things that people do, which is largely the result of their psychology. But I do think that events in the future are somewhat predictable because the implications of past behavior are somewhat predictable, and future behavior is somewhat predictable. The things that have happened, for example, in the economy and the markets are likely to induce certain behavior in the future, and you can know a little bit about that. When the history and the past behavior have been extreme, the ability to infer from that rises. In other words, I wrote a book about cycles, and that's the one where I refer to my experience traveling in India. Cycles are induced primarily by behavior—by exaggerating and overgeneralizing. In an upcycle, there's a trend line, let's say for GDP growth. But sometimes the economy grows more than that. Why? Largely, I think, because of optimism. Because people are optimistic, they spend a lot of money. Producers are optimistic about demand for their products, so they build new factories, hire labor, and buy machinery. The sum of these things produces a period of above-trend growth. But then, when producers have more than prepared for the coming growth and consumers have been sated by consumption, things will ease off, and maybe it results in a period of below-average growth. I was about two-thirds of the way through writing that book, and I've been studying cycles for almost sixty years now, living through them. I said to myself, "Just a minute, why do we have cycles? Why doesn't GDP, if the average growth in GDP is two percent, why is it not always two percent? Why is it three, or four, or one, or zero, or minus one?" The answer is excesses and corrections. You go to a negative, which is an excess of correction, and then it corrects back toward the trend line. Rather than thinking of cycles as ups and downs, which I think most people do, think of them as excesses and corrections, excesses and corrections—fluctuations around the trend line. This is most easily seen in the stock market, for example, when you look at the S&P 500. The S&P 500 has returned about ten percent a year on average for the last hundred years. But the constituents of the S&P have changed many times in that period. They do, but the average rate of return doesn't change. Average rate of return... I don't know about the S&P, but in India, the indices have returned about eleven percent over the last couple of decades. I'm a stock investor; that's my full-time job. We have currency depreciation of about four to five percent a year, so I assume the S&P would have returned six percent, if you were to ask me to wager a guess. But I don't think it's a fair benchmark because the companies in the S&P, like the indexes back home, change by virtue of market cap. They do. But my point is that the S&P, however constituted, has returned an average of ten percent a year for the last hundred years—but not steadily. In the '90s it returned twenty percent a year, and in the '00s it returned zero percent per year, so it's certainly not steady. Not only is that an interesting phenomenon to think about, but even more so is the fact that the return on the S&P, which averages ten, is almost never between eight and twelve. This is a very interesting datum. Why is it that the norm is not the average? Greed and fear? Excess and correction. An excess of optimism, a correction, and then an excess of pessimism. Can I ask you a small digression of a question? If humans were no longer in the picture and AI models were trading in the market, would it return ten percent every year, because there would be no optimism and pessimism? This is another one of those times when I'm going to say I'm not smart enough to know. However, it stands to reason that it would. If we programmed AI to find the elements and expect the patterns that produced success in the past and left it alone—and since we're told AI does not have greed, fear, optimism, or pessimism—you would think that the returns would be steadier. I think it makes sense to think that. I heard your interview a year ago with Nicolai from the Norwegian Sovereign Fund; he's a friend of mine. When he asked you, "Where are we in the cycle?" you said somewhere near the middle. Well, I think that was two years ago. Yes. Where are we now? Well, guess what? It's two years later. That's the only thing I know for sure. I believe that interview was in April of '24, and the market continued upward from April of '24 through, I think it's fair to say, January of '26. So another twenty-one months took it considerably higher. Of course, when people think of the age of the recovery, the economic recovery is two years older, and the bull market in stocks went on for almost another two years. First of all, as to the economy, it's very hard to date or talk about the age of this cycle because we had the pandemic, during which we did something unique: we closed the world economy to reduce the spread of the disease. That interfered with normal workings, and according to the people who make these decisions, it produced a recession. It was a very unusual recession because it was only one quarter. I always thought a recession was defined as two quarters of negative GDP growth. Of course, it didn't occur for the normal reason of excess optimism leading to a correction; it was man-made and was readily corrected. The second quarter of 2020 was the worst quarter in the history of the world for GDP, and then the third quarter was the best. So, was it really a recession in the functional terms of determining cyclicality, or was it just some noise? Can I ask another question here? If I were to consider GDP as the total production going into the economy, and consumers consume as much as we can, I could argue that a recession is truly when there's more production in an economy and the consumption is organically lesser. Do you think GDP is a good benchmark to figure out when there's a recession or not, since the consumers went away artificially back then? Well, I think your definition of a recession isn't quite right. What you said was, "there's more production, less consumption." In my opinion, the main element of a recession is actually a reduction of demand. Manufacturers are always happy to produce goods to meet demand, but a recession discourages them from producing. That reduced production is really what causes the recession—fewer people are at work, fewer goods are produced, and fewer are consumed. So in the pandemic, production went down, but the demand for consumption did not actually go down; the markets were just shut for a period of time. Because when they opened, people really consumed. Well, yes, the underlying demand didn't go down. People would have liked to have parties, they just weren't allowed to have parties. The psychological demand for a party was there, but the economic demand for rental halls, bands, food, and party dresses didn't exist. This is why I say that GDP was artificially depressed by a decision of government, really. So when we say, "How old is this recovery? Was that a real recession?" If that was a recession, then this recovery is now six years old. If that wasn't a recession, then this recovery is seventeen years old, which is far longer than the longest official recovery in history, which was ten years. Why seventeen? Because the last true recession before that was in '09. So it's been seventeen years since then. But can you just tie recessions and cycles down to tenure or time? You can't, and that's one of the reasons why I don't believe in predictions. But there seems to be a norm. We always said that the average was eight years for a recovery, and in the 2010s it went on for ten-plus years, so we called that the longest in history. Normally, if we have a recession in year X, we don't expect another recession two years later, because the recovery goes on for some period of years, which seems to average eight. I can't explain why, and I don't think anybody can tell you why. Maybe the answer is that eight years is long enough from the last one to permit the next one. I wrote the book about cycles, and somebody in England said, "Oh no, these aren't cycles because they're not predictable." They meant cycles like radio waves or sine waves, which are predictable. I think that patterns in the real world—the non-scientific, non-mechanical world—certainly aren't regular, but ups and downs do occur predictably. They may not be predictable in time or predictable in amplitude, but they certainly occur. So if I can bring you back, Howard, to the question: where are we in the cycle now? Well, I think the recovery has gone on a good while. It's not nascent, it's not adolescent, but it's also not geriatric. I think it's in middle age. I think the US economy, if that's what you're talking about, is healthy and doing quite well. If the thing that presages a downturn is excesses to the upside, you don't find too many of those right now. When I go to a city, I look for construction cranes in the sky to tell me if there's a building boom, because most booms are followed at some point by busts. I don't see that in most of our cities. I also don't see inventories growing. Inventories grow for two reasons: in times of weak consumption, because people don't buy the things that manufacturers produce, and in times of strong consumption, because manufacturers amp up production to get ahead of an anticipated surge in demand. Those often presage downturns when they turn out not to be warranted. I don't see that happening. Do you see it in some sectors? Well, maybe. I don't look at the data finely enough to tell you that. In AI, maybe? Pardon me? In AI, maybe? Oh, well, of course, we don't talk about inventory in AI, but there's certainly a boom in capital investment for AI. One could say hardware, energy, and compute is inventory. Yeah. Well, there's massive construction of infrastructure for AI, much like buildings and cranes. Yes, that's right. That is the area that's booming in our economy, and I need people who know more about AI than me to tell me whether it's going to bust. The question is: is all of this construction, or some of this construction, unwarranted? I don't think anybody can tell us that, but we certainly know it is an area of boom. How do you stay so sharp, Howard, at your age? I have watched a whole bunch of your interviews and read a lot of what you have written over the years. As a stock market investor all my life, I think there's so much I can learn from you. How do you stay this sharp today? Well, I'm lucky. I think I'm very lucky genetically. My dad lived to 101, and he was quite sharp at the end. We all face a decline at some point in time, and the only question is when does it start and at what rate does it progress? You look at Warren Buffett, Charlie Munger—some of my role models and friends—and they were or are sharp in their mid-90s and late 90s. So it's just luck. When you say "how do you stay sharp," I don't go to the gym and work my brain, but I do do a lot of puzzles, and I read. I read in fields that are not my fields. I'm still trying to gain knowledge and broaden myself. I guess I make some effort to plow new ground. The question is: when do you stop doing that, at what age? My last two memos—I hope your audience knows about the memos—were written on December 9th and February 25th, and they were about AI. I had to do a lot of learning. You changed your mind on AI as well? I changed my mind, but first I challenged my mind. For the second memo in particular, I had extensive conversations with Claude and learned a great deal. It was very exciting to be plowing new ground at almost eighty. Do you think it could be ambition and hunger? Well, it is definitely a hunger. It's a hunger to learn, to stay relevant, and to stay stimulated. When you say ambition, that usually starts with dollar signs. Not necessarily. Yes, well, it's an ambition to stay relevant and to stay sharp rather than starting to atrophy. I think staying relevant is the core ambition of everyone. The dollar is a means that people pick in the beginning, not at the end, maybe. I think most ambition starts with the dollar sign. In enlightened cases, it transitions to non-dollar. Not in every case—people are different. I think it was Mark Twain who said all generalizations are flawed, including this one. You simply can't generalize. Like you say very well—and I've heard you speak about the role luck plays in our lives—would you say they are enlightened or lucky, the ones who make that transition? Oh, well, now you're really getting deep because now you're talking about determinism versus intention. I think there is some of both. These things are hard to parse, Nikhil, but some people transition naturally. As they grow, maybe they become wiser and start to understand that there are things that are more important than money—or at least things that are important in addition to money. They do it naturally, not intentionally. I imagine there are also people who do it intentionally: people who go to the mountain, meditate, think about it, and conclude, "You know what? I really should change my focus at this point in my life." Have you been able to ever pull that off? I've been fascinated with the idea of going to a mountain and arriving at some kind of evolutionary thought in my mind that makes me actively transition. Have you been able to touch that? Well, I haven't physically gone to the mountain. But I think the key—and I've written the first pages of a book on this—the key to all of life is to behave thoughtfully, with your mind engaged. You can't just let the river take you. You have to give thought to: What's going on? Why is it going on? What does it mean? Why did it happen? What does it imply for me? What should I do about it? That is going through life with your eyes open. That's how you can intentionally get to a higher stage in life. The image that comes back to me is whether you let the river take you, or whether you try to figure out a better place to get to and then try to get there. Maybe once or twice in my life, I have felt like I could see the river. Can you, actively? I think at this stage, I am able to think about this progression constructively. When I think about my early decades—not years, decades—and what I did from the beginning of high school in 1960 until starting Oaktree in '95, I was adrift. During that thirty-five-year period, which took me up to about age forty-nine, I was adrift. I did not make proactive decisions; I let the river take me. I'm very lucky that it took me to some good places in several different aspects of life. But when I look back at my behavior, I don't consider it thoughtful or intentional. I'm just lucky that I got to a good place despite my own inattention. I think that starting Oaktree was the biggest and maybe first thing I really did with intention. I've tried to be more intentional since then. When I look back and describe how I selected my career, how I selected my first job, how I decided to go to graduate school, and how I transitioned from the equities department to the bond world in 1978—just in time to benefit from the birth of high-yield bonds—it was all happenstance. It was serendipity, coincidence, and passivity. I was not making intentional decisions in that period. What changed? How did you become intentional from being unintentional when you began Oaktree? Other people can maybe learn from it. Maybe I can learn how to catch it. Well, the easy answer is that my wife pushed me to think about it more and to independently start Oaktree. My partner, Bruce Karsh, and I did it together, so his participation encouraged mine. But that required a proactive decision. The biggest change I made before that professionally was moving from Citibank to TCW in '85. They approached me; that was not proactive on my part. Not the act of starting Oaktree itself, but moving from being adrift in the river to seeing the river. Well, I think once I made the decision to participate in the starting of Oaktree, I couldn't drift anymore. Now, I was the person leading Oaktree, and I had to make proactive decisions. It became my job, and maybe I warmed to it. Having made the proactive decision to start Oaktree and having done it, maybe I said, "Hey, this is good. I think that I... maybe I like leadership." Leadership is by definition proactive. It seems like an oxymoron to say someone led passively; those two words are in opposition. So I think that, thank God, I rose to the occasion and left TCW with Bruce and my other partners to start Oaktree. I don't think I ever said, "Well, now I'm going to start taking control of my life, now I'm going to start acting intentionally, now I'm going to become a leader." I just think it was inescapable. That's very interesting, because it sounds like you're saying entrepreneurship and taking risk can trigger the transition from riding the river to seeing the river. Some people are driven entrepreneurially. I think that entrepreneurship is the epitome of intentionality. It's taking the bull by the horns, as we say, proactively. The entrepreneur naturally, innately, does the opposite of drifting down the river. My inclination was not entrepreneurial. I liked most of what I was doing at TCW, but with encouragement from Bruce Karsh and my wife, and with a little encouragement from TCW to leave—in terms of how I thought I was treated—my inertia was overcome. That's how I would describe it. It wasn't a proactive decision: "Hey, let's get going." It was inescapable—the unlikely overcoming of my innate inertia. It's a very different story. It could be like a chicken-and-egg thing. Oh, it's very chicken-and-egg. Yes. But I think the important thing for the purposes of your audience is that my departure from TCW did not start with an entrepreneurial spirit. I know people who always dreamed of making a lot of money, always dreamed of running their own business, chafed at being an employee, and had to get out and start their own. That was never descriptive of me. So I did something that you might call entrepreneurial despite myself. That is the way I would describe it, rather than because of my innate drive. Howard, my job for twenty years has been that of a stock investor. You've been a debt person almost all your life. Why would you be in an asset class which has finite upside—and where the downside, I guess, is also finite because in most cases it's zero—versus equity where you could have exponential upside? Well, that's a great question, and I haven't really been asked that question much in the past. My answer to it may take a while, so settle in. The question as you pose it is interesting if you assume rationality and objectivity, but that ignores personal, let's say, kinks. I'll return to that. But first of all, my decision to move... I spent nine years in the equity department, so it wasn't really at the very beginning, though it felt like a long time. My decision to move from the equity department in '69 to the bond department in '78 was not my decision. It wasn't because I figured out it would be better for me. The Citibank I joined in September of '69 was an investor in what was called the Nifty Fifty—the fifty supposedly best and fastest-growing companies in America. These were companies where nothing bad could ever happen, and there was no price too high for the stock. If you bought those stocks the day I got to work in September '69 and held them tenaciously for five years, you lost about ninety-five percent of your money. Ninety-five percent! For many of them, something did go wrong, and for all of them, the price was too high. The P/E ratios, as I recall, were mostly between sixty and ninety. It was a real disaster for the people who invested in the Nifty Fifty, which was most of the money-center banks. I was Director of Research by the mid-'70s, so I was part of the process. We hired a new CIO who wanted to have a new head of research, and I helped him hire my successor. Then he said, "What do you want to do next?" I said, "I don't know, I could do this, I could do that." Typically, I didn't think it over very much or assess my strengths and weaknesses very well. He said, "I'd like you to move to the bond department and start a convertible bond fund," because he had come from a place that had one, it was very successful, and we didn't have one. I said yes. That was the extent of my intentionality, which was almost non-existent. I say now—and it may be an exaggeration—but I was lucky I didn't get fired. American companies gave pretty much lifetime employment at that time. Anyway, so number one, my move to the bond department was not voluntary, other than the fact that I could have said no. Number two, you talk about the fact that bonds have capped upside and some downside from default. That is certainly true. But it fit me because I was brought up to be very conservative. My parents were adults during the Great Depression. Now, your parents probably weren't alive during the Depression, and most people I know who are old had parents who were children during the Depression. My parents were adults during the Depression, which meant that when I grew up, what did I hear? "Don't put all your eggs in one basket," "Save for a rainy day," and "Avoid risk." I think Indian parents lived through pseudo-socialism in the country. Similar. Not as bad, but similar. Yes. But you, as a stock guy, look at bonds and say, "No upside, some downside." I say, "Predictable outcome," which is achieved almost every time. Is that true, though? Because a lot of the distressed bonds that you are in, which have fairly high coupons, is the chance of a downside zero? I said *almost* every time. First of all, as I said, I moved to the bond department in May of '78. In August of '78, I got the phone call that changed my life. A client has asked for a portfolio." Junk bonds. Pardon me? Michael Milken, junk bonds. Yeah, junk bonds. So I said yes, I can do it. That put me here today, forty-eight years later. What Malcolm Gladwell's book is really about, Nikhil, is that it's great to be at the front of the line. That's really what it's about. He talks about demographic luck—right time, right place. Somebody said, "Would you please stand in that line?" and I looked around and I was the first person in the line. You see, that's luck. Figuring it out is much harder. By the way, not all the people who were first in line were there because they figured out that was the line to be on. A lot of it is serendipity. And not all of them did well, either. That's right, not all of them did well. But anyway, the point is I've been in high-yield bonds for forty-eight years, and in our experience, ninety-nine percent of the bonds have paid interest and principal as promised. So I think I can say "almost every time." And if you earn on average ten percent, you only needed to be right ninety percent of the time. No, that's not right. If ten percent of your bonds defaulted and you lost ten percent of principal, even if you made ten percent interest on the ones that paid, you would have a zero return. You have to restrain your losses to a much lower figure than ten percent. You had to be right ninety-seven, ninety-eight, or ninety-nine percent of the time, and we were right ninety-nine percent of the time because I think we did it in an above-average way. The reliability appealed to me. I'm not a futurist or an optimist, and so I was well-suited to it. Then, of course, we got into the distressed debt business when Bruce Karsh joined me in '87. We brought out what I believe was one of the first distressed debt funds from a mainstream institution. He runs those funds and has managed about seventy-odd billion dollars since 1988 in that field, making him by far the biggest. Of his total profits and losses, well over ninety percent are profits, and less than ten percent are losses. So again, I think you can say "most of the time." The regularity and the contractual nature of returns on bonds appealed to me, and that is why I've been very comfortable in that field. The other thing is, frankly, a lot of your success in investing is determined by other people. If you go into a field that everybody likes, and they like it a lot and have bid up the price, then your returns are not likely to be high. If you go into a field where everybody else has been blind to the merits and says, "I wouldn't touch that with a ten-foot pole," and you figure out that it's good and it turns out to be good, that's how you get an exceptional return with low risk. For the most part, I was lucky at the time I made these decisions—actively or passively—and took these steps. I went into fields other people didn't like. You used the term "junk bonds," which is a derogatory term. Nobody talks about junk stocks, right? But junk bonds are much more predictable than stocks, and yet they call them junk. We'll call them distressed bonds. Yes, but that was a bias that made them available to me cheap. That was a very good thing, and I could see that and take advantage of it. Can you contextualize distressed bonds? Let's say the U.S. Treasury, the U.S. government, borrows at four percent. I read somewhere that Google is borrowing hundred-year paper at six percent, which sounds crazy, and we'll get to that. What kind of companies are in this distressed bond ecosystem, and what rate of interest are they paying? Well, we have to clarify. Oaktree has two businesses. One we call "performing credit" and one we call "opportunistic credit." That's the new euphemism for distressed. Wouldn't you benefit from calling it junk so you have a higher margin, because more people would stay out of it? Yes, if they listened to me and if I were that influential. That's the right kind of counter-thinking, but on the other hand, a big part of our business now is making loans to companies that need what we call "rescue loans." They don't like to be associated with the word "distressed," so we're more likely to get their business if we call it "opportunistic." Fair. In the performing credit area, we lend money to companies that the world thinks have a, let's say, three, four, five, or six percent probability of non-payment. We lend them money if we think it's actually one or two percent. If the world thinks they are five percent likely not to pay us, and we conclude they're only two percent likely not to pay us, then the world requires them to pay a rate of interest which is commensurate with a five percent probability of non-payment. This means if we're right and it's only two, we're getting something for nothing. That is what is called "excess return." That's what we do; that's the essence of lending money. Why would you lend money to a company that has a non-zero probability of not paying you back? Michael Milken's answer was that you can demand a rate of interest which is compensatory or more than compensatory. When you get payment in life for doing something which is more than compensatory, that's called excess return. So is the skill set at Oaktree calculating that spread, and how do you do that? Well, when you say the spread... the base skill set at Oaktree, the most important fundamental skill set, is predicting the probability of default better than other people. If we don't have that, we lack the necessary condition for superior performance. And is this skill set company-specific, or are you able to call the larger cycle in the market and hence do it better? No, that skill set is separate from the latter thing you talked about, which we call "macro." This skill set is micro. We have great analysts who look at companies, and we have a framework for analysis. The framework is essential—necessary but not sufficient. What makes it sufficient is having brilliant people to operate the procedure. The procedure itself is nothing without superior implementation, but we have both. Over the last forty years, on average, something like 3.6 or 3.7 percent of all high-yield bonds have gone into default every year, and our default rate has been roughly a third. That is our superiority, and that is a case-by-case, bottom-up, superior implementation. When you say superiority of the people running the process, and not just the process itself, do you mean attention to detail? Do you mean thinking outside of the box? Why do we have a lower default rate? Well, I think we have institutionally more experience than anybody else; we've been doing it longer. We also have a work environment that allows people to stay in that job for their lifetime. When I started at Citibank in '69, every analyst's goal was to become a portfolio manager because that's where the luster was. As long as that's the case, you don't build up institutional excellence on the analytical side because people are always trying to get out of that job. You want people to build up excellence and stay in the job so that you can get the benefit of their expertise. We set up that system so you can be a lifetime analyst and be very successful if you're great at it. That is one very important thing. So we have people who have been doing it for years and years, decades, and we have the benefit of their expertise. We have the institutional experience which gives us stability in times of rocky psychology. When people get too excited or too depressed, we tend not to—and that is a great help. We have a process which enumerates eight areas of investigation and many subsidiary questions within that, and everybody has to follow it. When I started doing something like this at Citibank as Director of Research, people said, "Oh, you're taking away our creativity." But we told them they had to follow a process, but they could follow it creatively. The process consisted of covering the bases. You have to cover the bases consistently, asking the same questions of every company, every time. In the equity world where you live, you'll get a research report that says, "Buy this company, great management," but it doesn't talk about the product. Or, "Buy this company, great product," but it doesn't talk about management. "Buy this company, great tax shelter," but it doesn't talk about either. I felt that it was important to cover every base every time. When you look at a portfolio and you see a stock that's down eighty percent, it's usually because something wasn't covered—because the analysis was not disciplined enough to touch all the bases. Again, that highlights the difference between stocks and bonds. We're bean counters. We are green eye-shade types, but at least we do it well and consistently. The equity investor has imagination, foresight, and entrepreneurial spirit, but doesn't analyze things as consistently as we do. Well, look, I wrote a memo nine or ten years ago called "Investing Without People." It was pretty early days. When I went to graduate school at the University of Chicago in, let's say, '68, the professor said most mutual funds underperform the S&P, and they charge high fees. It was really... so my answer is that indexation has taken over not because it's so good, but because active management was so bad. But I believe that people can do some things that AI can't. Could you also say that indexation and ETFs have taken over because the markets have been in an uptrend largely? In a more volatile time, active might make sense; in a depression, active might make sense. You know what? The bad times create an opening for active management, but it's still hard, and not many people can do it well. Why do bad times create an opening? Because panic drives down, let's say, stock prices. But that same panic causes most people to panic, which means most people can't step up and buy in the panic, you see? There was a guy named Wally Deemer, who was an old-time trader who had some great quotes and turned them into a little book. His greatest quote of all was, "When the time comes to buy, you won't want to." There was a phrase in our world—a friend of mine in England used it as the title for a book: "Simple but not easy." Our job is simple. What makes the better founders available to you rather than somebody else? Charlie Munger once spoke to me about this concept of "simple but not easy." When I finished my first book, *The Most Important Thing*, I had lunch with Charlie. As I got up to leave, he said, "Just remember, none of this is easy. Anybody who thinks it's easy is stupid." That was one of the greatest things anybody ever said to me, delivered in his typically brusque way. It can't be easy to be smarter and more disciplined than all the other smart people who are trying. That's what the efficient market hypothesis says: all these smart, highly motivated, educated, numerate, computer-literate, interconnected people are trying to get rich. He's also intolerant of my not staying sharp and moving ahead. You know, how do you keep moving ahead at 80? That's really the question, but I don't think it's impossible. A great example—the example that immediately comes to mind, and I think it's a great one—is that he observed, and I wrote in the memo "Something of Value" in January of '21 as I recall, he pointed out something almost revelatory: readily available quantitative information about the present cannot hold the key to success because everybody has it. Success in investing is doing better than others. Investing is a funny field because it's really easy to be average and it's really hard to be above average. But readily available quantitative information about the present is not going to make you above average for the simple reason that everybody has it. I talk in the memo about how one of the reasons Buffett was able to steal a march on everybody else and become Buffett is because he sought information out and worked it when nobody else did. When I was starting off in this business, in the research world, think about your process. Let's say I said, "You know, I went into a store today, I bought a product, I like the product, I think maybe that company has a future, and I'd like to learn about it." How would you get information? Think about what you would do if you had that experience this morning here in New York City. What did we do back then? You know what we did? We wrote a letter to the company and asked for a copy of the annual report. It took a week for them to get that letter. They put it at the bottom of the pile, and when they took the letter off the top of the pile, they sent that person an annual report. It took a week or two to get down to my letter, and then they took an annual report, put it in an envelope, addressed it to me, put a stamp on it, put it in the mailbox, and it took another week for it to get to me. Now, I exaggerate, but the point is it took a month to get an annual report so that I could start studying the company. There was no online; there was no Wiki or whatever source you might use. The only alternative was these books called Moody's Manuals, and each book was this thick. You would take it off the shelf. It had paper called onionskin—the thinnest of paper—and it had tiny print, tiny print; you needed glasses. They had the current financials on every company. There was not much narrative, nothing, no discussion of the future, but just the financials of every company. That's all you could get until your annual report came in the mail. Then, maybe you could start figuring out the business and its potential. So you could, if you were Buffett and sat in your office in Omaha and read the Moody's Manual when nobody else was willing because it was stultifying, get an advantage. "Is that what he did?" "I think, well, that's my illustration of what he did. Maybe he did more, I don't know." "I'm no one to pay you a compliment, Howard, but if I could..." "Compliments are always accepted." "I think the nimbleness and the willingness you have to change at 80 is by far the most impressive thing about you. When I speak to really successful people, I think this trait over all others kind of makes them who they are." Well, I appreciate that comment. I hope it's true. But it makes sense, doesn't it? The ability to change your mind, change your thinking, and learn new things is an example or a component of superior insight, which is what you have to have. This is a competitive game. It's like golf. If I say, "Today I shot 74," you have no idea if I won or lost. The only thing that matters is: what did everybody else shoot, right? If I tell you last year I made 13% in my stock portfolio, is that good or bad? Well, it's pretty good; the average return on the S&P is 10%. But of course, the S&P was up 18% last year, so 13% stank. So this is a competitive game. It's not a matter of being right or wrong; it's a matter of being more right than the other person, or less wrong. "Are you able to change because you have been able to consciously remove ego?" Well, I think that helps. If you have too much of the wrong ego, it would probably, I would imagine, tend to make you say, "I've always done it this way, it's worked in the past, I'm an enormous success, and I'm worth this much money, so why should I change my approach?" But you have to keep changing. Innovate or die. "What could trigger it? If we were to advise people watching this, how can they trigger the realization that they need to constantly change?" Well, you know, it's a funny thing. This has been a theme for me for the last nine months. I say this every time I get a question like yours, Nikhil. The really hard questions are the ones that start with the word "how." How can I learn the need to evolve and grow? How can I learn to see things better, more clearly than other people? How can I learn to be a credit analyst who better understands the default probability than anybody else? How can I become what I call a "second-level thinker," understanding things at a higher level and better than other people? And all the "how"s, I can't tell you. How can I remain sharp at 80? I can't tell you. I can tell you what you have to do—you have to do all the things that I mentioned in order to be a superior investor—I just can't tell you how to do it. In my first book, which is called "The Most Important Thing," it's funny; when I had the idea for the book, I put in 21 chapters. Each chapter says, "The most important thing is..." and then it's a different thing because there are so many things that are important. I told Columbia University about the book, and they wanted to publish it. So they said, "Give us a sample chapter." I wrote out a chapter. Each chapter says "The most important thing is..." and this one said "Second-level thinking." I wrote the chapter about second-level thinking. I had never really thought about it before; it just kind of struck me and I wrote it down. What it says is pretty simple: If you think the same as everybody else, you'll act the same as everybody else. If you act the same as everybody else, your investments will perform the same as everybody else. So, to perform better, you have to deviate from the herd. That seems clear. But how do you do it? How do you do it? In the book, I said that asking me how to become a second-level thinker is like the saying in basketball: "You can't coach height." All the coaching in the world will not make your team taller. So, I can tell you that you must become a second-level thinker to be successful as an investor. I just can't tell you how. "If I were to draw an extrapolation that second-level thinking is also contrarian thinking in a way, do you think AIs, which are predictive in nature, can be modeled to be contradictory tomorrow?" But that's the challenge, you see. Because second-level thinking, or contrarian thinking, is not just different from the herd. That's not enough. It has to be different from the herd and better. So you could say—I don't know how to program AI, but I assume you can say to Claude—"Every answer you give me on every company has to be different from the consensus." "Or the opposite of consensus?" Well, the opposite is pretty categorical. But let's say it has to deviate from the consensus. "No consensus thinking is welcome here." You can say that. You can make it be different. But can you make it be better? This is the catch, because the people you compete against in the investment arena are also intelligent, educated, literate, computer-literate, and highly motivated. They're pretty good. Much of the time, they're as close to right as you can get. So if you believe the consensus is as close to right as most people can get, and you tell Claude, "I only want non-consensus thinking," then it has a high probability of being wrong. That's not a good thing. "In a way, AI and investing could work if everyone in investing uses AI." No, I don't think so. Because if everybody uses AI, and let's assume everybody's AI is the same... "No, some different." Well, are they? That's the question. Maybe that's what my partner Bruce Karsh, the recovered lawyer, would call a gating question: Are some AI models smarter than others? Since they all have super IQs, computing power, and they all are trained on the same history, are some smarter than others? I don't know. I need somebody to tell me. But if they're all equally smart and you say to them—and this is an interesting thing—everyone says to all of them, "Write out a process that will produce investment success." Theoretically, they all write the same one because they are all equally smart and have the same training. If they all follow it perfectly, they all produce the same result. So there is no superiority. "But then everybody makes 10%; nobody makes 8% or 12%." Right, but what do you get paid for making 10%? "But AI doesn't need to be paid." Yeah, but who employs AI? That person wants to get paid. Nobody is employing AI for fun, right? They're employing AI because they want to get rich. And getting rich in investing comes from superiority, not from averageness. "How do you get superiority? I don't want to ask you this question, but—" "But you will." "You can choose not to answer it." "Yes. Do you think the world is going to socialism and AI is triggering that?" I don't consider myself a futurist. I don't engage in that kind of thinking; that kind of thinking is what we call blue sky. I am partial to what Albert Einstein said on the subject. He said something like, "I never think of the future; it'll come soon enough." "But do you think about the past?" I think about the past to learn from it. I'm great on these quotes, and my favorite on this subject is from Mark Twain who supposedly—though I think he didn't really say it—said, "History does not repeat itself, but it often rhymes." There are certain themes that rhyme from instance to instance in history, and they mostly have to do with human nature. Human nature changes incredibly slowly. When we talk about fight or flight, people start talking about the stone-age man and the watering hole, right? Well, that was millions of years ago, but those things are still relevant. The point is that you can learn from history, and what you mostly learn are the lessons of human nature. "Mark Twain said that, and Napoleon said history is written by the winners of tomorrow, so we don't even know how accurate the history we have access to today really is." "Well, except we have the numbers." "Financial history, yes." "Financially, when you have numerical history, it's probably not that subjective." "But maybe not the psychology that led to the numbers." "Right, right. Well, I mean, I just read Andrew Ross Sorkin's new book, '1929.' It's a great book. I have a memo coming out this week talking about private credit, and I refer to it in that." "What's happening with private credit?" Well, let me just finish this thought, okay? He explains the events that led up to the Great Crash rather vividly and gives you a picture. I think there's a lot to be learned from that, and I try to convey some of the lessons of 1929 in this new memo. I hope that the history that Andrew described is the real history, because if not, the lessons will not be that valid. That's all I can say. "Any last words, Howard, to our audience? Any piece of advice?" Well, what I always say to people about investing, Nikhil, is that investing is a puzzle—a challenging puzzle. I define investing as positioning your capital to take advantage of future developments. But I also say that future developments can't be predicted accurately. So, you need superior insight to get it more right than most people. But you're not going to get it right all the time. If there's something in your makeup that says you're going to be unhappy if you're not right all the time, don't become an investor. Taleb, in his book "Fooled by Randomness," talks about the difference between investing and dentistry. He says if you go to dental school and learn how to fill a cavity, and you fill the cavity that way every time, you'll be successful every time. If you have to be successful every time, become a dentist or a civil engineer. You say, "I want to build a bridge from here to there," you do the calculations—how much steel, how much concrete—and every time, the bridge stands. But it's not that interesting, in my opinion. Investing deals with this fascinating puzzle that you're trying to solve to a better degree than others, even though there are no laws that work absolutely. "Fascinating. And I love it, and it's still a puzzle I'm trying to peel like an onion." But you're never done, and that makes it interesting. That's the kind of person who should be engaged in it. "It's interesting that you resonate with Taleb, because somehow when I read your stuff and his stuff, I find you guys on the opposite ends of the spectrum. I think when Taleb ran his fund, he was buying way out-of-the-money call options and put options hoping for black swan events, and you in a way are doing the opposite. You're buying distressed debt, hoping that the percentage of things being black swans is lower than what the market perceives." Right. Oh, absolutely. In that sense, I think we are different. I said earlier that I believe in putting together a portfolio that will do well in the main portion of the probability distribution. He's advocating protection that can be bought cheaply because it only deals with a tail event which rarely happens, so people underestimate the value of that tail protection. You can cheaply buy protection against something that is unlikely to happen—which in theory is a good thing—but since it happens so rarely, the value of that underpriced tail protection doesn't become apparent very often. Even he compared—in the book, as I recall, and I only read it 22 years ago or something like that... "I've read all his books, I love him." Yeah. But even he says that investing is like Russian roulette, but rather than having a bullet in six chambers, there are 100 chambers in the gun, so the bullet rarely goes off. I don't think you can—I mean, you could spend 1% of your money to buy tail protection every year, but if that's going to be your whole existence, it's going to be a dull existence because the explosion will happen so infrequently. "And he also said that even COVID was not an explosion. He said it's not a black swan event." "So if COVID wasn't, the question is: what is?" Well, yes. I mean, obviously COVID was not the first pandemic, but I would say it was the first pandemic of the modern era. It was the first pandemic that encountered the digital economy, globalization, and many other things. So it was unique and certainly innovative in a lot of ways. I would guess we could say some people dealt with it better and others worse, which is to me the definition of what makes something interesting: the ability to deal with it better or worse. "Thank you, Howard, for taking the time today. This was incredibly interesting, and I feel like I'm learning so much from you." Well, I have learned from speaking with you, and I've enjoyed it. Your questions were, in many cases, novel and provocative. "Thank you." "Thank you for doing this. Cheers."

Stanford Leadership Forum 2026: Conversation with Ken Griffin

Stanford Graduate School of Business(2.7M)·45:42·10.2K views·2026-05-04
Ken GriffinFounder and CEO, Citadel Amit SeruProfessor of Finance, Stanford GSB
TL;DR"The biggest difference between Citadel and [large global…
Leadership in a volatile, AI-driven era requires a relentless commitment to meritocracy, the agility to abandon "sunk costs" immediately, and a focus on lifelong learning to stay ahead of rapid technological shifts that are eroding traditional competitive moats. The...

TL;DR: Leadership in a volatile, AI-driven era requires a relentless commitment to meritocracy, the agility to abandon "sunk costs" immediately, and a focus on lifelong learning to stay ahead of rapid technological shifts that are eroding traditional competitive moats.

Leadership and Decision-Making Under Uncertainty

  • The "Sunk Cost" Fallacy: Successful organizations avoid the trap of sticking to a failing direction to save face.
      • At Citadel, the culture focuses on admitting mistakes quickly ("Ah, we screwed that up, next") rather than burying them in paperwork, a common failure in large global banks.
      • Flexibility as a Competitive Edge: Leaders must be willing to "snap the neck" of an organization by changing direction the moment information shifts.
  • Resilience in Crisis: Griffin describes the 2008 financial crisis as a "near-death experience" for Citadel rather than an academic exercise.
      • Key Lesson: The crisis revealed the danger of non-bank institutions relying on wholesale funding markets that can vanish instantly. The primary takeaway was "don't pretend to be a bank unless you are a bank."
  • Learning from Failure: History is written by winners, but Griffin emphasizes studying the chapters where things went off the rails, citing Steve Jobs' failures (like the Apple Newton) as essential components of eventual success.

Market Integrity vs. Crony Capitalism

  • Crony Capitalism vs. Merit: Griffin argues that favoritism and "state capitalism" lead to inferior products and lower standards of living.
      • Merit-Based Success: American prosperity is driven by first-generation entrepreneurs (Bezos, Zuckerberg, Huang) who built trillion-dollar businesses from nothing, the antithesis of an oligarchic society.
  • The Executive Voice: Corporate leaders should speak publicly on issues where they have credible expertise—such as immigration, tariffs, and antitrust—but should generally stay out of social issues to avoid alienating the public.
  • Healthy Competition: Griffin views underperformance (e.g., losing to competitors over a six-week period) as a vital catalyst for continuous improvement and internal reflection.

The AI Revolution and the Erosion of "Moats"

  • "Agentic AI" and High-Skill Jobs: AI is no longer just providing a 20% boost to software engineers; it is now performing work previously reserved for PhDs and Masters-level researchers in hours rather than months.
      • Griffin notes this shift is "unsettling" because it targets high-level research and white-collar productivity rather than just entry-level tasks.
  • Erosion of Competitive Moats: Traditional barriers to entry, such as massive data centers or distribution networks, are being neutralized by cloud computing and AI.
      • Entrepreneurial Opportunity: It is easier than ever for small startups to challenge incumbents.
      • Example: A pet insurance startup recently sold for $1 billion after using AI image generation to identify dog breeds from social media posts and deliver personalized marketing to new owners.
  • Human Capital: In an AI world, the primary goal of education must be "learning how to learn," as specific technical bodies of knowledge will depreciate faster than ever.

Education and the Future of the American Dream

  • The Crisis in K-12 Education: Griffin expressed deep concern over the state of U.S. public schools, noting that in some districts, not a single student is proficient at grade level in math.
  • Historical Illiteracy: He argues that a functioning democracy requires a populace that understands its history. He cites a lack of understanding regarding the failures of socialism as a threat to the "last beachhead" of personal liberty.
  • Giving Back: Griffin emphasizes an "intergenerational spirit of giving," where successful leaders have a high expectation to contribute time and resources to their communities and mentor the next generation.

Career Advice for Future Leaders

  • Surround Yourself with "Non-Clones": Effective leaders do not hire people like themselves. They seek diverse perspectives (different backgrounds and life experiences) to create the tension and debate necessary for better decision-making.
  • Passion vs. Wealth: Pursuing wealth for the sake of wealth leads to disappointment.
      • Greatness requires a combination of talent and intrinsic motivation.
      • Griffin encourages graduates to find work that resonates "deep in their soul," as that durability is required to survive the "roller coaster" of high-stakes leadership.
"The biggest difference between Citadel and [large global banks]... there’s no sunk cost here. Where I worked, everyone would try to figure out who to blame for the mistake and how to bury it. Here, it’s just like, 'Ah, we screwed that up, next.'" — Ken Griffin
"The issue here with AI is that in the world of AI, humans were the horses." — Ken Griffin (citing Niall Ferguson)
"In life you will only be great if you’re both talented and passionate. If you think you’re going to pursue just wealth for the sake of wealth, you’ll be profoundly disappointed... find what resonates with you deep in your soul." — Ken Griffin

Of note, competitive moats that large companies have depended upon are going to be filled in with AI tools. So the ability for small companies to take on incumbents will be higher than ever. You know, a friend of mine had a... this is so this great story... pet insurance business. And he he turned the keys of the business over to his 25-year-old son to run.
